13 August 2018

The fund prices quoted in FTfm are supplied by the operator of the relevant fund. Details of funds, including prices, are for informational purposes only. The Financial Times Limited makes no representation as to their accuracy or completeness, and they should not be relied upon when making an investment decision. The sale of interests in the funds listed in FTfm in certain jurisdictions may be restricted by law and the funds will not necessarily be available to persons in all jurisdictions in which the publication circulates. Persons in any doubt should take appropriate professional advice.

Data collated by Morningstar. For all other queries, contact the FT at reader.enquiries@ft.com. The fund prices published in this edition along with additional information are also available on the Financial Times website at "http://www.ft.com/funds". Charges for this advertising service are based on the number of lines published and the classification of the fund. Please contact data@ft.com or call +44 (0)20 7873 3132. The funds published on these pages are grouped together by fund management company.

Prices are in pence unless otherwise indicated and those designated $ with no prefix refer to US dollars. Yields % allow for buying expenses. Prices of certain older insurance linked plans are subject to capital gains tax on sales. Some Property Unit Trusts are limited to investors who are UK tax exempt.

All dealings are subject to individual Trust Deed rules. The sale prices for these funds are estimates.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in theUK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors .

Single price: Based onamid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available .

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information. contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge fromfund managers/operators.

* Indicates funds which do not price on Fridays.

Other explanatory notes are contained in the last column of the FT Managed Funds Service. Prospectus data, price histories, charges and risk analytics on the funds within these pages is available online at "http://www.ft.com/funds".

Elga Bartsch has moved to BlackRock as head of economic and markets research for the BlackRock Investment Institute (BII), the global research platform of the world's largest fund manager. Ms Bartsch has worked for Morgan Stanley for 20 years, most recently as chief European economist and global co-head of economics. Tara Rice also joined the BII as deputy head of economic and markets research in June. Ms Rice previously held a senior role at the Financial Stability Board. BlackRock has also hired Ludovic Phalippou, a finance professor at the University of Oxford Sad Business School, University of Oxford, as an adviser on private markets investing. Rajat Agrawal has also joined BlackRock as a managing director for its Asian private credit team. Mr Agrawal, who will be based in Mumbai, previously worked for DBS India, the financial services provider.

markets investing. Rajat Agrawal has also joined BlackRock as a managing director for its Asian private credit team. Mr Agrawal, who will be based in Mumbai, previously worked for DBS India, the financial services provider.

Hawaii's $16.5bn Employees' Retirement System has appointed Elizabeth Burton as chief investment officer of the state's public pension fund. Ms Burton, who previously worked for Maryland State Retirement and Pension System, replaces Vijoy Chattergy who left in February.

Standard Life Aberdeen, the 610bn Edinburgh-based investment manager, has hired David Lam as head of Hong Kong. Mr Lam joins from Carret Private Investments, a multifamily office based in Hong Kong.

Rob Arnott, the entrepreneur known as the "godfather of smart beta", has stepped down as chief executive of Research Affiliates, the California-based indices and data analytics provider. Katrina Sherrerd, the chief operating officer, has assumed the role of chief executive. Mr Arnott will remain in his role as chairman.

State Street has named Andrew Allright as chief executive of InfraHedge, the $30bn hedge fund managed accounts subsidiary of the US listed bank. Mr Allright previously served as head of client solutions.

KPMG, the professional services provider., has hired Bill Packman as a partner in its UK wealth management team. Mr Packman previously served as chief operating officer at Nutmeg, the online wealth manager.

Martin Visairas is returning to Old Mutual Global Investors, the 30.7bn London-based asset manager, as managing director responsible for institutional clients. Mr Visairas previously worked for Citigroup as global head of capital introductions.

Scott Troeller has joined BlueMountain, the $20bn New Yorkbased alternative asset manager, as a private infrastructure portfolio manager. He previously worked for Fir Tree Partners, another New Yorkbased private investment manager.

Markets
Fintech Crowd Dives Into Subprime Credit-Card Lending; Financial-technology startups are stepping into a void increasingly left by credit-card-issuing banks: lending to customers with poor credit histories

By AnnaMaria Andriotis and Peter Rudegeair 
686 words
13 August 2018
20:39
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Financial-technology startups are stepping into a void increasingly left by credit-card-issuing banks: lending to customers with poor credit histories.

LendUp Global Inc. and Fair Square Financial LLC, which focus more heavily on riskier borrowers, mailed out roughly 35 million credit-card offers during the first half of the year, according to market-research firm Competiscan, up from 7 million during the same period last year.

CreditShop LLC, a specialist in personal loans to risky borrowers that was acquired last year by investment firm Vrde Partners, rolled out a credit card earlier this year. Elevate Credit Inc., which specializes in high-cost installment loans, launched one in July.

Subprime lending can be lucrative. Most of these cards carry interest rates north of 20%, significantly higher than the average credit card interest rate of 14.1%, according to the Federal Reserve. Rewards programs, one of the biggest costs for large card issuers chasing creditworthy customers, are rare.

But risks abound: Facing rising loan losses, especially among the riskiest borrowers, banks are reining in their growth in this sector. Subprime credit-card balances at seven large U.S. banks rose 3% in the first half of the year from a year prior, down from a 13% increase in the year-earlier period, according to Autonomous Research. Capital One Financial Corp.'s subprime balances accounted for 32% of its domestic credit-card balances in the first half of 2018 compared with 36% in the same period a year earlier

The new lenders are getting help from some industry stalwarts. The Orogen Group, an investment firm headed by former Citigroup Inc. chief Vikram Pandit, said in May it was committing $100 million in equity to Fair Square, which distributes cards to borrowers with less-than-pristine credit scores. LendUp recently announced that Capital One co-founder Nigel Morris and former Capital One chief credit officer Frank Rotman were joining its board of directors.

The population of subprime borrowers "is nigh on half of America, and there's enormous opportunity for others to be able to offer a great product with great sophistication to compete in this space," Mr. Morris said in an interview.

Fintech startups still account for a relatively small slice of the subprime-card market. At the end of 2017, Fair Square had 124,000 open accounts, more than half of which went to customers which prime credit scores at the time they were opened, and just shy of $95 million in balances for its Ollo card holders, according to people familiar with the matter.

Capital One has around $32 billion in subprime credit-card balances on its books.

The new entrants say their use of machine learning and artificial intelligence for underwriting helps them manage the risk. They also mostly extend small credit lines, often ranging between $500 and $2,000, limiting the scale of potential losses.

Around 60 million U.S. adults have credit scores lower than 650, according to Fair Isaac Corp., roughly the threshold where banks focused on prime borrowers stop lending. Some 53 million U.S. adults don't have credit scores at all because they have little or no borrowing history.

Fintech lenders have been targeting both groups, often using data not included in applicants' credit reports, such as how often they change addresses and whether they pay their utility bills on time, to determine whether to approve them.

Fintech startups looking to enter the credit-card business have a few challenges that banks don't. They typically must partner with banks to issue the cards on their behalf to comply with rules set by card networks Visa Inc. and Mastercard Inc., and they need a source of funding to lend against. LendUp, for example, uses Transportation Alliance Bank to issue its Arrow Card and has a $100 million line of credit from Victory Park Capital Advisors LLC, a Chicago-based investment firm.

Pro Private Markets
Vista Could Raise Nearly $14 Billion for Latest Fund

By William Louch 
580 words
13 August 2018
16:36
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Technology-focused private-equity firm Vista Equity Partners could raise nearly $14 billion for its seventh flagship fund, pension fund documents show.

This would put Vista in line to become one of the latest private-equity shops to raise its largest-ever fund.

A document published on Aug. 8 by Oregon State Treasury, which invests money on behalf of employees of Oregon State, said the fund is expected to be 20% to 25% larger than its predecessor, which raised more than $11 billion in 2016.

WSJ Pro reported in May that Vista was seeking to raise $12 billion or more for its latest fund.

The Oregon Investment Council said in the document that it recommended an investment of up to $500 million in the fund. Vista's latest buyout fund will seek to take majority stakes in 15 software and data companies worth between $750 million and $10 billion, the document shows.

The fund has yet to set a hard cap despite returning to market earlier this year, the document shows, the latest sign of the increasing clout top-performing private-equity shops have in today's buoyant fundraising market.

Soaring investor demand means buyout funds can increasingly dictate terms during fundraising, in some instances not letting investors know how much they will seek to raise until the firm has held an initial or final close. Investors typically like to know how much a firm plans to raise at the outset so they don't find themselves backing a fund that is a lot bigger than expected.

Vista only set a hard cap on its previous fund after holding a first close in March 2016, WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported.

Other large-cap firms including Advent International Corp., Cinven and Apax Partners also began marketing their most recent funds without establishing an upper limit, WSJ Pro said in the same report.

Raising larger funds has become increasingly common in recent years as yield-starved investors turn to well-established private-equity brands in a bid to boost returns.

Carlyle Group LP recently raised $18.5 billion for its latest flagship buyout fund, it said in a statement in July. Last year, Apollo Global Management LLC raised $24.7 billion the largest pool of capital ever raised by a private-equity firm.

The Oregon State document lists Vista's increasing fund size as one of several concerns.

One of the challenges it says it expects Vista will face is increasing competition for deals.

Rival private-equity firms and sovereign-wealth funds are attracted by the strong recurring revenue software businesses generate. Last year, deals for technology companies accounted for about one-fifth of all acquisitions globally, Dealogic Ltd. data shows.

"Vista was a first mover in a nascent market for software buyouts but & as new competition has arisen, there has been a step change in purchase prices for mature and profitable software companies," the document says.

Vista was founded in 2000 by former Goldman Sachs Group Inc. executives Robert Smith and Brian Sheth.

The firm manages over $31 billion and has backed companies, including educational-software provider PowerSchool Group LLC and data-analytics provider TIBCO Software Inc.

Since inception, the firm has invested approximately $19 billion in more than 70 deals, generating a net internal rate of return of more than 20%, the document says.

Pro Private Markets
Footwear Designer Vionic Explores a Sale

By Jaewon Kang 
413 words
13 August 2018
11:30
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

A private equity-backed footwear designer is marching toward a potential deal.

Vionic Group LLC has engaged Robert W. Baird & Co. to weigh a sale, said people familiar with the matter.

The Alpine Investors-backed company is projected to generate more than $30 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization in 2018, the people said.

Founded in 1979 by podiatrist Phillip Vasyli, the San Rafael, Calif.-based business designs supportive shoes, sandals and heels with the aim of melding style with footwear technology. Vionic makes so-called biomechanical footbeds that are designed to provide extra sole support. The company sells its shoes online and through retailers such as DSW Inc., Zappos.com Inc. and QVC Inc.

Alpine invested in Vionic in 2012. As an Alpine portfolio company, Vionic has rolled out new product styles and boosted its direct-selling capabilities by establishing its own stores, the first of which opened last year. It also appointed the former president of Deckers Outdoor Corp. unit UGG, Connie Rishwain, as its president in 2016.

"We're always exploring strategic and financial opportunities to help fuel our growth, but there is no active deal in place. We will continue to evaluate all our options to determine what is best for Vionic's exciting future," said Chris Gallagher, Vionic's chief executive. He didn't comment further.

The apparel-and-footwear category is becoming increasingly competitive. The retail market continues to evolve, driven by changing preferences among shoppers looking for unique fashion items. Makers of functional apparel and footwear with proprietary technology have fared better than the broader sector.

The U.S. footwear segment expanded retail sales 1.6% in 2017, generating $74.15 billion, compared with $72.97 billion in 2016, according to data provider Euromonitor International Ltd.

Recent private transactions in the space include Charlesbank Capital Partners' purchase of "https://www.wsj.com/articles/charlesbank-to-buy-bankrupt-rockport-for-150-million-1530729995?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=3" out of bankruptcy protection and Optimal Investment Group's acquisition of USA Dawgs Inc.

Alpine invests in companies in the software, consumer-services and business-services industries. The San Francisco firm backs North American businesses with $2 million to $25 million of Ebitda, at times dipping down to consider companies in the software sector that generate at least $1 million of Ebitda.

Alpine in April "https://www.wsj.com/articles/alpine-investors-backs-comlinkdata-1523548850?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1" in Comniscient Technologies LLC, a provider of data analytics software that does business as Comlinkdata.

Business
Why One of the Biggest Mall Owners Is Doubling Down on Malls; William Taubman of Taubman Centers says the mall isn't dead. It's just changing.

By Christina Binkley 
1,601 words
13 August 2018
03:01
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

While mall owners have faced record store closures and bankruptcy filings as more Americans shop online, Taubman Centers Inc. has doubled down.

It is reinvesting in design, technology, big-data tools and new store mixes for its 24 malls. One of the biggest overhauls is a $500 million revamp of the Beverly Center in central Los Angeles.

The Bloomfield Hills, Mich., company is adding natural light, a chef-curated food hall and new luxury tenants. Taubman is also bringing in services for high-end clients through an armada of personal shoppers and the London-based concierge service Quintessentially, as well as analyzing consumer data with OneMarket, a retail-technology network.

Big-ticket moves such as these have been controversial. Some shareholders pressed for less-expensive strategies, and the dissatisfaction contributed to the election of activist investor Jonathan Litt to the company's board in May, replacing William S. Taubman, son of the company's founder. Mr. Taubman, known as "Billy," continues in his role as the company's chief operations officer.

Mr. Taubman argues that some of his competitors have spent much more Westfield Corp. recently spent $1 billion revamping the Century City Mall in Los Angeles while others are failing to invest enough in the future of shopping.

Mr. Taubman sat down with The Wall Street Journal to discuss his strategy. Here are edited excerpts of the conversation.

A mall overhaul

WSJ: Let's begin with a bird's-eye view of what you're trying to accomplish with the redo of the Beverly Center. You're in a competitive shopping market, with the Century City mall having just finished its own very extensive makeover. And Rodeo Drive is 10 minutes away.

MR. TAUBMAN: Let's go to 30,000 feet. I think what's most interesting about Beverly, and is representative of the future, is that Century City really focused their entire development around the department stores. The basis of our plan was everything but the department stores. We basically did nothing with the department stores. We just did something with everything else [such as mixing brands, and adding new food choices, signage and client services].

WSJ: Your reinvestment strategy has been controversial with some of your shareholders, and may have contributed to your replacement on the board in May. To what do you ascribe those investors' concerns?

MR. TAUBMAN: Reinvesting in Beverly Center was a generational decision necessary for it to keep its competitive position in one of the most important and growing markets. The property needed to be rethought and reimagined in order to remain relevant to the L.A. consumer and our significant Asian tourist business. The friction comes from the fact that investors are focused on short-term returns, but this transformational reinvestment will maximize the long-term value of the asset.

WSJ: Why do you think this reinvestment strategy will be successful at a time when retail is generally experiencing something of a recession?

MR. TAUBMAN: Destination retail centers continue to improve and gain market share. They are not in a recession. Our efforts to reinvigorate the center are already paying off. As we mentioned on our recent earnings call, we're seeing materially improved traffic and sales productivity. Even with the project not yet completed, its sales per square foot has grown by double digits. If this trend continues, sales will reach historic levels this year.

WSJ: You're focused on a lot of brand stores smaller and more mixed. Is there still a need for anchor stores at malls?

MR. TAUBMAN: I use this analogy about the anchors. In the '60s and '70s, the three television networks ABC, NBC and CBS had like a 90% market share. Today, they probably have a 20% market share or a 15% market share, but they represent the cultural consensus of America. As opposed to cable, which is narrowcast.

Retailing has shifted like cable. There is still a function for the [traditional networks] it's just not the function it was. When my father was building shopping centers, anchors were guaranteed a certain sales performance and draw of customers because everybody did shop at the anchors consistently. That's now less true.

WSJ: How do you figure out what those narrowcast needs are in your shoppers?

MR. TAUBMAN: We did a year of market research. We embedded a videographer and an anthropologist into 40 different homes for five days. We videoed the customer as she made every decision. We lived with them to really understand why and what they were doing, and why did they come to Beverly or Century City? We saw that the decision making was complex. Food was incredibly important in her decision making. That's why we were so focused on the food at Beverly.

A full experience

WSJ: Everyone's talking these days about experiential retail. What does that mean for your malls?

MR. TAUBMAN: We're putting [clothing retailer] Canada Goose in [the mall in Short Hills, N.J.] They'll put a cold room in, it'll be 12, 13 degrees. You'll be able to put coats on and walk into the rooms to test out what they feel like. Indigo, we're bringing it in from Canada it's a 30,000-square-foot bookstore. The cooking department has a chef every evening and on the weekends, cooking, demonstrating. And we're bringing in [fashion retailer] Brunello Cucinelli. We haven't announced everybody we're bringing in, but there's just a whole bunch of new luxury retailers.

WSJ: Luxury brands often want to be segregated from everyone else.

MR. TAUBMAN: The luxury tends to want to be in its own [area in the mall, but they do] want to be near Apple. With the Beverly Center, we moved Apple up to the seventh floor; it's right next to the luxury zone.

WSJ: Would you like to mix it up more?

MR. TAUBMAN: I would and I wouldn't: I do think that the Gucci shopper goes into Zara. Now, they buy different things in Zara than they buy in Gucci. On the other hand I have to be careful what I say here there are many midprice women's apparel stores that I actually don't think the Gucci shopper is shopping at. So, do I think she goes into Footlocker to buy a pair of shoes? Absolutely. She's not trying to replace her Christian Louboutins with Nikes, but she wants Nike as well, for another part of her life. So, I think that if the association makes sense for the customer, then it'll make sense for the retailer.

WSJ: You also get a lot of tourists at the Beverly Center. How do they even find it?

MR. TAUBMAN: We've been adopted by Chinese tourists, and we had a whole strategy for them. About nine years ago, we hired the marketing consultant that had helped Louboutin in China. We employ Chinese speakers in the information booth and take Union Pay [the Chinese international credit card]. Every Chinese student who arrives in L.A. for university gets a welcome package from Beverly with special benefits and an invitation to come see us and have a concierge to help them. Chinese students, we find, get a lot of family as well as friends, so there's a real spending opportunity with everybody who comes over to see them.

WSJ: Are you adding new services for shoppers?

MR. TAUBMAN: Of course, we have Wi-Fi everywhere. That's sort of the price of admission today. In the Beverly Center, Quintessentially is our concierge service.

What happens is, you're a Chinese billionaire and you decide that you want to come to L.A., you want courtside seats for the Lakers, you want Michael Mina to cook you a meal, and you want a stylist. Your concierge will be able to plan that.

WSJ: Is there any other infrastructure technology that you're looking at for the near future?

MR. TAUBMAN: We've signed up with Westfield for OneMarket. If OneMarket is successful and we're betting on it we believe it will create a marriage of the virtual and real world that will allow us to address customers one on one and make their shopping experience far more efficient. Ultimately, the major retailers will all have their inventory on the system, using big data.

So, when you get in your car and you say to Waze or you go into an Uber and you say you want to go to the Beverly Center, we're going to know that you've pinned a black dress on your picture app. And we'll be able to tell you, "By the way, that black dress is on the second floor at Bloomingdale's and we'll have Louise pull it out right now and put it in a fitting room, and we will take you directly to it."

WSJ: How soon do you think that's going to be real?

MR. TAUBMAN: I think two to three years. We're testing now.

Ms. Binkley is a writer in Los Angeles. She can be reached at "mailto:report@wsj.com".

News
Silicon Valley's fatal attraction to Saudi Arabia

TomBraithwaite 
588 words
11 August 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Main
11
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

S ilicon Valley royalty received a visit from the real thing. Google co-founder Sergey Brin and venture capitalist Peter Thiel were among those meeting Crown Prince Mohammed bin Salman during his US tour in April. The budding relationship was on display again this week. The FT revealed that Saudi Arabia's wealth fund had spent $2bn to acquire almost 5 per cent of Tesla's stock. The mutual attraction is obvious. The crown prince has an agenda to transform his oil economy into an investing and tech powerhouse. For their part, tech groups, especially those burning cash, appreciate patient investors.

Yet the Saudi tech splurge is far more than meets the eye, while the glaring contradictions and obvious potential for disaster are swept under the carpet.

First, the sprawl. Japan's SoftBank has drawn a lot of attention for the size and volume of investments in tech, but remember that almost half of its $100bn Vision Fund is Saudi money. That means companies as diverse as Slack, the messaging app, and SoFi, the online lender, have Riyadh to thank, in part, for their surging valuations. WeWork, the shared office provider, received another $1bn this week; so widely is the fund sprayed around that the news barely registered.

For some companies, it goes further. Magic Leap, the consumer virtual reality company, has a direct investment from the Saudi Public Investment Fund. At Uber, the PIF acquired a 5 per cent stake in 2016 before SoftBank bought a 17 per cent stake for $9bn last year. That adds up to enough financial clout for real influence. Indeed, the PIF's Yasir al-Rumayyan sits on the ride-hailing company's board. Those are just the ones we know about. The Tesla revelation shows that Saudi Arabia is prepared to invest quietly in sizeable amounts which stay just below the disclosure threshold of 5 per cent. No other foreign government is close to building such a position in US tech.

US companies should not need reminding that the behaviour of this government is as distant from Californian values as it is possible to imagine. When Uber gobbled up the first investment from the PIF, women were still barred from driving in the kingdom. Only months ago, Saudi businessmen were locked up in Riyadh's Ritz-Carlton.

For all the crown prince's liberalising agenda, he leads a repressive regime waging a war abroad and beheading people at home. Given this, the embrace of investment by Silicon Valley's liberal elite might be their biggest act of hypocrisy yet.

No such qualms are expected from the Trump administration. But these are sensitive industries. SoftBank has stakes in companies such as Nvidia, the chip company for artificial intelligence and virtual reality.

Stability is not guaranteed on either side. The Trump administration likes to blow hot and cold, even with allies; it has used its powers to block foreign investments, especially from China.

From the Saudi side, it was striking that officials   annoyed by the Canadian foreign minister voicing support for jailed rights activists   ordered their overseas asset managers to sell Canadian holdings. How soon could they tire of a tech sector which is slow to reciprocate with the hoped-for inward investment in Saudi Arabia? Whether this spending spree ends in boom, bust or fire sale, it will be something to behold.

tom.braithwaite@ft.com

Public or Private, Tesla Fans Are Along for the Ride; Many retail investors say they plan to stick with the electric-car maker for the long haul; one is 'betting on the jockey, not the horse'

By Michael Wursthorn and Asjylyn Loder 
1,195 words
10 August 2018
13:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Plenty of Tesla Inc.'s die-hard holders are prepared to stick with Elon Musk, no matter if the electric-car maker is "https://www.wsj.com/articles/teslas-big-question-better-or-worse-off-as-private-company-1533751228?tesla=y&mod=article_inline".

"As long as it's an option for me to invest my money alongside Elon, I'm going to," said James Stephenson, who says he has been a Tesla investor since 2014.

Whether individuals and some institutional investors will be able to stay in Tesla will depend on how a deal is structured, though. Some holders of Tesla stock could face hurdles to participating.

Mr. Musk has said he wants "https://www.wsj.com/articles/elon-musks-twitter-account-am-considering-taking-tesla-private-at-420-1533661152?mod=article_inline&mod=article_inline", giving investors a choice: stay with the company or sell their shares and receive $420 cash for each. The more shareholders who opt to stay, the less cash the company or any new investor would have to pony up.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/experts-cast-doubt-on-musks-envisioned-buyout-of-tesla-1533680027"for a deal"https://www.wsj.com/articles/teslas-go-private-dream-doesnt-add-up-1533677506?mod=article_inline". Tesla, which has dwindling cash reserves and continues to generate negative free-cash flow, hasn't given any detail on how it would fund a buyout.

Mr. Musk tweeted "funding secured," but a later statement "https://www.wsj.com/articles/teslas-board-has-met-several-times-to-discuss-going-private-proposal-1533736367?mod=article_inline" was less definitive. It said a meeting with Mr. Musk a week earlier had "also addressed the funding for this to occur."

Individual shareholders like Mr. Stephenson hold about 12% of Tesla's stock, according to FactSet, and many say they won't go. The 40-year-old financial analyst who lives in Florida said he wants to hold on to his Tesla shares, which he says number 169 and were worth around $60,000 based on Thursday's close.

"I'm not going to sell at $420, and most other Tesla shareholders I've spoken with aren't going to sell either," said Mr. Stephenson, adding he expects Tesla to reach a $1 trillion market capitalization over the next decade, versus around $60 billion today.

Mr. Musk said in "https://twitter.com/elonmusk/status/1026890900885184514"that he would create "a special purpose fund enabling anyone to stay with Tesla," but the car maker and Mr. Musk haven't elaborated on how such a vehicle would work. Usually, individual investors who don't meet certain income and asset criteria face steep regulatory barriers to participating in a buyout.

What's more, almost 8% of Tesla shareholders are index funds that follow benchmarks that exclude unlisted companies, according to Morningstar. Most of those would likely have to cash out since they can't generally hold stock not included in public markets or an index.

An additional 24% of shareholders are mutual funds and exchange-traded funds that face limits on holdings of securities that don't trade easily. In most cases, they can't have more than 15% of their portfolio in illiquid securities.

These funds, which are among Tesla's biggest shareholders, have yet to say how they would react to a go-private transaction. T. Rowe Price Associates, Tesla's second-largest holder after Mr. Musk, with a 9.2% stake, declined to comment. So, too, did No. 3 holder Fidelity Management & Research Co., with an 8.16% stake.

Baillie Gifford, the next largest holder, said in a statement: "As long-term shareholders, we will take time to reflect upon this development."

Vanguard Group, which is Tesla's sixth-largest holder, owns shares mostly through index funds, which would likely have to sell. "Our active funds are technically able to invest in private companies, but it is rare for them to do so," a spokesman said.

Some smaller fund managers say they are on board. Craig Blanchfield, a portfolio manager at Mosaic Advisors, a money manager that oversees more than $241 million, said he wants to continue to hold Tesla shares. He said Tesla has already been a lucrative investment for him, as well as clients for whom he manages money.

Mr. Blanchfield added that Mr. Musk's penchant for success from revolutionizing payments with PayPal Holdings Inc. to building rocket company Space Exploration Technologies Corp. is a key reason behind his plan to hold Tesla shares indefinitely. "This is an example of betting on the jockey, not the horse," said Mr. Blanchfield.

The $174.7 million Ark Industrial Innovation ETF, an actively managed fund from Ark Investment Management, is another committed bull. Tesla is its largest holding, accounting for 11.7% of the fund's portfolio, according to FactSet. Two other Ark ETFs own smaller stakes in Tesla.

"We think that $420 is a very low price to pay for Tesla today," said Tasha Keeney, an analyst with Ark Investment Management. The fund would prefer Tesla stay public because liquidity limits might push it to sell, but she argued that the market is underestimating the growth of battery-powered cars.

Factoring that in, Ms. Keeney estimated Tesla's shares could reach $4,000 in five years.

The scant detail provided by Mr. Musk has given pause to some steadfast supporters. Galileo Russell, a 25-year-old founder of a financial media startup, HyperChange, geared at millennials, views a take-private move as a "step backwards in terms of liquidity and transparency."

Mr. Russell conceded heavy short selling in Tesla stock, which entails investors betting a share price will decline, has been a distraction for the company, as Mr. Musk has said.

Investors who have placed wagers on Tesla shares falling have accumulated about $12.5 billion in short interest, making the electric-car maker one of the most shorted stocks in the U.S., according to Ihor Dusaniwsky, head of predictive analytics at S3 Partners.

"I'm not super convinced going private is the right move," said Mr. Russell. Still, he says he doesn't plan to sell any of his 60 shares, valued at about $21,000. "The financial upside could be huge. I'm holding this for 10, 20, 30 years at least."

For Prem Kalevar, a 29-year-old Canadian entrepreneur, Tesla's long-term potential isn't enough to mask liquidity concerns. Mr. Kalevar said he would sell a meaningful portion of what he says is his low six-figure holding that he has accumulated since 2013 if Mr. Musk takes the company private.

"Given the complexity and uncertainty of holding private company stock, my willingness to hold as large as a position as I do now would go down," Mr. Kalevar said.

 Miriam Gottfried contributed to this article.

Write to Michael Wursthorn at "mailto:Michael.Wursthorn@wsj.com" and Asjylyn Loder at "mailto:asjylyn.loder@wsj.com"

Economy
Transcript: Audience Q&A With Fed's Thomas Barkin in Roanoke, Va. Richmond Fed chief talks about cryptocurrencies, cyberrisks and U.S. tensions with trading partners

5,529 words
10 August 2018
17:42
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Federal Reserve Bank of Richmond President Thomas Barkin answered questions from the audience during an appearance in Roanoke, Va., on Wednesday, Aug. 8, 2018. He discussed cryptocurrencies, cyberrisks, the opioid crisis and U.S. tensions with trading partners, among other issues. Here is a transcript of the exchange, lightly edited for clarity and length.

THOMAS BARKIN: (In progress) ... I focus on three big things. I spend a lot of time digging into the economy. As a guy who had a business background, I'm deeply respectful of economic thinking, but maybe not as fluent as folks who do it every day. I thought the only thing I was going to bring to the FOMC was to make sure that I had learned and be able to talk about economics in a way that was credible around the Federal Open Market Committee table. (Inaudible)   in and of itself. I have been digging hard into economics and the economy.

The second thing I've been doing is digging into the district. And as I said, I've been everywhere now. (Inaudible.) But West Virginia's the place where we're going next month. But just trying to understand what's happening in cities and towns that   and some of the stuff I talked about today, the urban/rural split, that's not something I spent time thinking about six months ago, but it's something I spend a lot of time thinking about today. So trying to understand what's happening in our district.

And then the third part, I'd say, is just developing my own voice. As a competitive guy, if you show up around the FOMC table with the group of talented colleagues that I described, you want to be able to add value. And, you know, I'm hopeful that two things   that being able to talk about economic issues in the way that firms think is a distinctive value-add   (inaudible). So I've been pushing more on trying to do that.

Q: OK. How does the information that you learn through events and outreach opportunities like this help inform your view on monetary policy?

MR. BARKIN: Oh, gosh, it helps a ton. So one of the challenges we have is we're trying to make really important decisions off of data that is by and large three months old and always revised. So, for example, we found out that the savings rate   three weeks ago we found the savings rate actually hadn't been 3.6 percent for the last year; it was 7 percent. So actually 3 percent to 6.8 percent, you know, shocking changes in the data. And so you don't want to go into the room, look at the data, and make a decision without trying to understand what's happening on the ground.

So one of the kind of things that my colleagues and I spend a ton of time doing is trying to test what we think we're seeing in the data with what's happening on the ground. If wage increases look modest in the data but you tell me they're massive on the ground, then I start asking some questions. If price increases look significant in the data but you say not that meaningful on the ground, fantastic. So I like to think that what we're doing is constant testing of what we see with what we hear. And I think the two of those put together give us the best chance to get an accurate view of what's happening in the economy.

MODERATOR: Along those lines, I imagine the questions that you have or look at maybe   (inaudible)   get questions when you come to Fed meetings, conferences   (inaudible)   several questions. The last time it was the way that you look at wages, but   (inaudible)   seeing turnover, wage inflation. So that gives us an opportunity to hear more about what specifically is going on at that time, so.

MR. BARKIN: And I'm going to add, we meet every six weeks. You might not think that the economy changes that much every six weeks, but I'd say every time we meet there's new stuff on the table. In January, it was the tax cut. In March, it was the tariffs. And I might be wrong, but I think in April it was the Italian fiscal situation and what that meant for others, wages. I mean, so there's always something. So it's incredibly helpful to stay abreast of what's happening in the market, and why I appreciate   (inaudible)   today.

MODERATOR: So, Tom, with regard to the economy, what keeps you up at night? (Laughter.)

MR. BARKIN: So, you know, we are many, many years into an economic expansion that, you know, started slowly but I think has started to gain some traction in the last couple years. I've been told by many people that economic expansions don't die of old age, they die of cardiac arrest. And so I'm trying to figure out what's the shock to the system that could, you know, put a stop or   (inaudible). You know, is it what's happening internationally? Is it a cyberrisk of some sort? Is it, you know, financial conditions? There are certain markets that are   that are leveraged or overleveraged.

But I think it's a hard job trying to forecast the future. And you know, if you decide that there's going to be a shock you're only right roughly one out of five times. But I do try to spend time thinking about what's around the corner, you know, that could put the brakes on what's been, I think, a very   (inaudible)   virtuous circle.

MODERATOR: And if it's not a   not a cyberattack or something spectacular, where do you think the   where do you see the next recession could possibly come from?

MR. BARKIN: Again, I think that confidence matters a lot in our economy. I think   if you go back to '09 to '12, I think there were businesses that, you know, for whatever set of reasons, weren't that confident in investing in   (inaudible). And I think we're just now seeing businesses start to make those investments. We're seeing investment come back. And I think that this   (inaudible)   on business confidence would be   (inaudible)   in consumer confidence. You know, do you feel like you've got a job, you've got enough money that you could spend a little money on your family or on yourself? Or do you feel like you're   (inaudible)   paychecks   (inaudible)? So, again, things that have to do with consumer confidence would be the same.

So, you know, I'm very focused on confidence and things that drive our confidence because if you take consumer spending, you could easily   and we've seen it happen   make a change in spending to saving like that if you start to feel nervous about the future. Businesses could   (inaudible)   shareholders   (inaudible)   the future like that. And so it is a wide range of things that could affect it, but I focus a lot on the confidence.

... And consumer savings rate   you know, in addition to everything going down, businesses hunkered down, consumers hunkered down, people lost their jobs. There's a vicious cycle and a virtuous cycle. In '08, you saw the vicious cycle of people getting cautious all at the same time, which then, you know, made the economy contract even more.

MODERATOR: One more question. You touched on tariffs. Any idea where this might   the outcome might be on tariffs?

MR. BARKIN: No. No. So I think the most important thing to say right now is, you know, we're in the middle of a negotiation with a bunch of countries that could land on the spectrum all the way from fairer trade deals with a lot of, you know, countries that is a boost and a stimulus to the economy; to modest tariffs in many places that don't really hit the bottom line of the economy quite significantly; to a significant trade war that most economists say would be detrimental to the economy. I don't think anybody has any idea where it's going now ...

We're not seeing massive issues with economic performance today across the entire economy driven by, you know, the various trade   (inaudible). For sure, you know, we've had tariffs on steel that are driving prices up. For sure, these threatened tariffs on soybeans are driving soybean prices down. ... But it's a pretty big economy here, and across the whole economy I don't think we see a massive impact. So we're in that middle state, but it could go left or it could go right. We certainly don't know, or I certainly don't know, so.

Q: My question is regarding tariffs   (inaudible). How much of an impact does unfair trade practices impact the future of   (inaudible)?

MR. BARKIN: So, again, economic theory would suggest   (inaudible)   theory about free trade that presumes fair trade, all right, as opposed to unfair trade. Some of our predecessors made calls a number of years ago in the context of various trade agreements or in the execution of those trade agreements to allow things that might not have been as fair to continue. I presume that they did it because they thought net-net it was still better than not having the trade at all. And, in fact, I have looked at it in depth; my instinct is many, many, many of these trade deals that are currently situated have benefitted us, right, net, even though they may not be as fair as they could be.

That said, we have a totally legitimate interest to making them as fair as they could be. And I think if they were fairer, they'd be better. So I wouldn't go back and say we'd be better off if we hadn't traded with China or Mexico. I don't intuitively believe that's correct. But do I believe we'd have better trade if it was fairer? I do.

Q: Hi. Good morning. So the question   a lot of technologists and futurists are predicting that robotics and artificial intelligence, automation   (inaudible). Are you guys having a conversation around this topic? And, if so, what are your thoughts on how it may affect the economy?

MR. BARKIN: I've seen a lot of the same studies you are and, you know, I'll say that there's a lot of studies. I'm reminded of the   Time magazine had a study in the early '60s where they looked at the effect of automation and they decided if we could just get all the automation implemented, we would all just work a three-hour day. (Laughter.) And that's actually what they thought, and then the picture was the robot butler that would scoot over and give you your drink at the end your three-hour day. (Laughter.) And so technology is going to come. Technology will replace some jobs. It will   you know, industries are constantly being disrupted by technology.

A little bit the question is, will that happen   (inaudible)? And there's a piece to it which is positive for the economy, that I think a lot of businesses   (inaudible)   more effective. It will create new jobs. Like AI   artificial intelligence translators is a job that's going to be quite a big job in the future that actually doesn't exist today. But the key to making it really work in the long term is for our workforce to be capable enough that they can redeploy in the way that economic theory suggests they do.

And that's why I think workforce development is such a big thing. And if you read books, James Hills's great book I would recommend, and it talks about some of the challenges that workforces have in redeployment. You definitely get the feeling that technology will come in and displace workers, but if we don't have any thought to how those workers redeploy to other tasks, that that's our real issue as an economy.

And as I said before, probably right now we have jobs for everyone who's looking for jobs. We need to get some of the folks who aren't looking for jobs to be, you know, back in the workforce looking for employment   (inaudible). And so I think we do have an opportunity to be investing in the development of that workforce to be able to do that.

Q: To follow on just a little bit on that, lately world-wide, particularly in emerging economies, there's an increasing awareness of the value added for investing in (research and development), including fundamental or what's called basic research, whether it be in biomedical, engineering, communications, whatever it might be. It seems like in the last decade we've lost a bit of will, and perhaps policy can help guide us back to that.

I suppose there are two choices, and I wonder what your opinion is. One choice is we become a country that takes advantage of fundamental discoveries made elsewhere in the world and translate and apply. The other is that we double down on the history of the United States to be a world leader in fundamental discovery. Can policy from where you sit affect that decision? And what is your opinion about which way we should go?

MR. BARKIN: The way you described the second one sounded a lot better than when you described the first one. (Laughter.)

I'll probably give a non-answer to the question, but, you know, policy-wise   (inaudible)   how just, you know, or where the government spends money on whatever. I do have a feeling that the right spending on infrastructure for this country is an important thing. And infrastructure has many different components to it.

You, obviously, think about roads and rail lines and those things as physical infrastructure, but I think that basic research is part of the fundamental infrastructure base of this country. And I do think that investment in basic research by this country has paid off in the past and would continue to do that going forward.

I'm also more than comfortable with us taking advantage of other people's innovations and innovating on top of those ourselves. So I don't   I don't see it as much as a choice between the two; I see it as a both/and.

Q: (In progress following break) ... The Fed is a regulator, and in the recent past it seems that one size fits all has been more prominent. What can the Fed do to encourage the growth and the success of local small banks?

MR. BARKIN: Well, there are a couple of things in there. But I think   I think the core of what you're asking is, do we think that one size fits all makes sense for our oversight and support of banks? And I think you'll see in some of the regulatory stuff that has passed recently and in the support that we've given to that regulatory stuff that I, and I think others, are supportive of tailoring.

The way I think about it is I was an ex-consultant, so they should be getting the same protection of the economy with less administration, less ask. That's a very attractive thought to me. And so I think it's a very attractive thought to say, horses for horses, we should be tailoring recommendation to what the actual risk is and what the actual need is. And I think you see that coming out of the most recent Senate bill. We're in the process of, you know, tailoring our reviews by size of institution and by risk of institution to make sure we give the most in-depth reviews to the ones that have the most risk.

I'd just say as an aside that, to me, there's actually a lot of interesting stuff in entrepreneurial finance. A lot of it's online and, you know, what's happening in some of those fintech firms, they're getting to that as well. And I do think for the smaller banks, you know, how they compete with the online thing is another   a big issue. And I've noticed the (Office of the Comptroller of the Currency) has just reached out to start regulating some of the online banks. I think that will be a good thing as well.

MODERATOR: I think that's   from a banker's standpoint, that feels like they have a playing field, that they come in without regulation and yet we're regulated.

MR. BARKIN: Well, again, we're always trying   you asked about what keeps me up at night. We're always trying to think about what the next problem is, not the last problem. I actually do have a philosophical   or view that we never make the same mistake twice. But what is the next problem? And so parts of the system that are underregulated or unregulated, I think that those are places to look at.

Q: Talk to us a little bit about cryptocurrencies, and how you see it impacting our economy and on the future.

MR. BARKIN: We'll see. (Laughter.) I have to report my investments every year, so I want to make clear that I am not invested in any cryptocurrencies. (Laughter.)

So the whole   the blockchain methodology, which is behind cryptocurrency, is a really interesting methodology providing verification and security. And most of the folks that I have talked to believe that's a real innovation and has real value.

The question of whether that is best used as a currency is an interesting one, and I think what I would think about it is this. Obviously, if you got some currency and someone else will pay you for it, then it's currency. But if you value currency as   if you value the stability features of currency   you know, money has a stable source of value   then it's hard for me to imagine how you would be using as currency something that isn't backed by the government, but is instead backed by a bunch of people who, by definition, you don't know. It just doesn't make that much sense to me. And so I think it's a fun investment, but I don't know that it's yet got the level of protection that you would hope for in currency.

Q: You've done some talking about sort of the next problem and other economic disruptions. Can you talk a little bit about environmental factors? For example, sea-level rise. Many of the primary economic cities that you represent   Richmond, Baltimore, Norfolk, Hampton Roads   they're all affected by sea-level rise. And I just wondered what part, if any, that it played in environmental resiliency.

MR. BARKIN: I am a homeowner on the beach in South Carolina, so I'm aligned with your concerns. Also, offshore drilling.

Actually, the Richmond Fed just published a paper   I think I saw it; it was yesterday or the day before   on climate change and some of the impact on the economy. And I think I'm being direct, though; our sense of the economy is more a two- or three- or four-year kind of look, and I think a lot of these climate-change issues are much longer-term kind of issues. And so it's not obvious to me that the rising of the   potential rising of the seas is something we can take into account in next month's interest-rate decision. And so I just think that is what it is.

There are some interesting analyses that have been done and are being done. And I would point to this thing Richmond   and talk about, you know, what the economic impact could be of higher temperatures, higher   (inaudible)   you know, those sort of things.

And, of course, there are puts and takes. You create a   it's hotter for you, but it's, you know, nicer for somebody in Canada is sort of puts and takes. But I wouldn't say we do a ton on that.

It is one of the interesting things about being in the Federal Reserve is, you know, our lane is pretty focused. The Congress has given us the task of setting interest rates, maximum employment, stable prices, and that's where we focus. There's actually a huge economy out there that we don't control. Things like fiscal policy, policy makers have a lot more influence over that than we do. We have the chance to comment on it, but we really don't control it.

Q: Health care is a significant, growing part of the (gross domestic product) in this country. And I've heard some economists say it's unsustainable more than worrisome, crowding out other economies. Other economists are saying thank goodness for health care and we obviously   (inaudible)   more and more. Where do you come down on that?

MR. BARKIN: I don't think I have an opinion on whether health care should be a bigger sector of the economy or a smaller sector of the economy. I have heard lots of stuff; I've talked to lots of people ... And the economists I speak to, you know, do find the funding system of the health-care model to be sort of counter   (inaudible)   economic theory with cross-subsidies, as you know, from government-funded to, you know, charity cases, people who   (inaudible)   insurance. So there's a lot of belief in the   in the profession that if we could, you know, find a way to clean up the payment models and make it a more efficient payment model, that might have benefits for everyone ...

But I don't think we have an intuitive   I don't believe this is a managed economy. I don't believe we'll ever wake up and say health care ought to be bigger and something else ought to be smaller. I'd like to see the markets work.

Q: What is the   your influence and difference in influence between a credit union and a bank?

MR. BARKIN: Well, I mean, credit unions are regulated by different operating machinery than banks are. You probably know that. They have a different tax regime. And so, you know, our   we are regulating banks that we regulate; we're not talking about their oversight.

I'm not sure what you're   is that what you're getting at?

Q: Well, basically, the difference in the operations of the   of the two, if you   if you influence at all credit unions.

MR. BARKIN: I don't think we do, meaningfully. I mean, I think they're   they're regulated in their lane, but we're not in their oversight.

Banking regulation is very complicated, a whole  nother conversation, but there are multiple regulators in the banking space. We regulate   (inaudible)   banks. We regulate bank holding companies. That's what we've been chartered to regulate. And there are other regulators   the OCC, the (Federal Deposit Insurance Corp.), for example   that regulate other institutions. And we do our best to try to keep it all aligned. We certainly regulate the biggest banks, right? We don't regulate all the small banks. In fact, there are multiple regulators in that space.

Q: I thought it was interesting that you were responsible for overall (information technology) for the Fed. And Susan, when she asked you about what keeps you up at night, you brought up two things, and Susan also brought up catastrophic cyber problems. I was wondering, then: Do you feel that business owners big and small should be devoting more resources towards cyberrisk prevention? And, if so, where would you start, based on your knowledge of that?

MR. BARKIN: So, sadly, I do think we should be spending more resources on cyber loss prevention. And I say that knowing that those resources don't increase productivity, and I said productivity is important to growth. But sustainability is also important to growth, and I think this is one of those things that's about sustainability.

By the way, we looked at this much in my prior world as a consultant, too, and so it's something I'm relatively familiar with. And the most important thing and I think the place to start as a business is, what information do you have that, if released to other people, would be damaging to your business or more broadly? And so the answer will differ by businesses. There are businesses that really don't have very much that matters. Obviously, a lot of retailers have credit-card numbers. You may have sensitive personal information for some of your employees or maybe even some of your customers. But understanding really clearly what's the stuff that really matters and doesn't matter.

Then the challenge is that most people think of cyber prevention as the technology. And there's something to be said about the technology, making sure that your firewalls are secure and your patches are clear. There are firms out there   (inaudible)   firms out there will do independent assessments and try to penetrate your firewalls and give you a good sense of how vulnerable you are.

The real issue you've got is human error, and human error is a massive issue. Almost all of major recent cyber things that have been in the public   think the stuff that happened in the last election   were phishing attempts that some human, who's really busy and has a hundred emails and just trying to get through the day, clicked on a link and in many cases put their password into the link, which enabled bad guys to get access to the internal systems.

And so, you know, one of great challenges   now, the good thing is this doesn't have to cost a lot of money. The bad thing is it requires a very fundamentally different   (inaudible)   culture within an organization. So it's a cultural thing that says, actually, this is a huge competitive issue to us.

You know, I used to say in my old firm that people hired us in part because we were so trustworthy personally. They were going to trust us with their secrets and their strategies, and we had to be as trustworthy technologically as we were personally   which is a really hard thing for people to get their heads around because they have great ethics and great reputations, but they don't necessarily understand what it means to be trustworthy technologically. It is different than it means to be trustworthy personally. So I really do think it's an imperative for everybody.

You may have noticed   (inaudible)   company last week talked about risks in the energy sector. And I think there's a lot of folks   a lot of bad guys out there who are targeting it, and I think it's probably the most contemporary way to steal from you. And so, you know, just like banks, you know, the armed guards, you know, you need to think about the same approach with your business.

Q: Can you speak to what the Fed is seeing in the   (inaudible)? Certainly, it's starting to occur on the West Coast   (inaudible)   years. I'm not in   (inaudible)   but it appears there are some real problems with housing or a crisis. New construction prices are going up rapidly because of   partly because of tariffs and other issues. Can you speak to how the Fed's going to   (inaudible)?

MR. BARKIN: Now, I'm   (inaudible)   in Richmond today, I'm reading prices are ridiculous.

Here's how   here's what I think is happening in the numbers. It may be different by market. Coming out of the last downturn, new home construction, you know, went through the floor and it's been very slow to come back. Part of that was we were overbuilt beforehand. Part of it was confidence. Part of it was access to funds, right? But it's been slow to come back. And I think today a large part of it is access to trade, laborers. You know, what I'm told is it's really hard to get construction workers these days to build new homes. And if you do, it costs a lot of money to do it.

At the same time, new home demand has started to rise, and part of that is you have more confidence in the economy. I think a large part of it is there's a whole millennial generation that, you know, the story was five years ago they were going to live in the cities and bike to work. And a lot of them are   they're getting older, having kids, and buying houses. And so I think home demand has gone up at the same time that home construction has been slow to come back. And, of course, what comes out of that is an increase in housing prices. That's, I think, a relatively simplistic answer to what's happening.

Whether that's a bubble and what the impacts of the bubble are is worth a conversation. I mean, one definition of a bubble is, are prices higher than the long-term rates would imply? And I do think it's market-dependent, of course, but there are many markets where that's true. In our role as regulators and also overseers of the economy, we try to ask the question of whether banks are overexposed. That's the kind of bubble we had in '07-'08. It wasn't just pricing; it was also financial market overexposure.

And we do not have any sense that that's the case right now. In fact, like I said, if there's anything we've worked hard to prevent, it's overexposure, you know, in bank balance sheets. So we may be wrong, but we're pretty comfortable with that right now.

MODERATOR: Any other questions?

One last question, if I may. Could you comment on the opioid crisis and also on student loans? I hear that is a   is that an issue? Is that a concern   (inaudible)?

MR. BARKIN: I think both are   both are concerns.

On the   for us, we see the opioid   our team in Richmond has done a lot of research on it. We think hard about, you know, what it takes to get folks into the labor force. I talked about our district. Labor participation in our district is in the low 60s   61, 62, 63 (percent)   in every state in our district except for West Virginia, where it's 51 percent. So there's 10 percent fewer parts of the population who are looking for work or working. And I'm told a lot of it has to do with, you know, the opioid issue. So I'm also told by a lot of folks who are trying to hire tradesmen and truck drivers that they can't pass a drug test, and so that's another inhibitor. And so doing something to get, you know, good folks clean so that they can work I think is a huge   (inaudible)   for our economy. And I'm sure it's true in multiple markets around here, too.

On student loans, student-loan debt is high, and we do watch that closely. I think the question is student-loan debt as a percentage of total consumer debt or as a percentage of the total economy is not a huge number, so we don't at this point see massive risk to the economy for student-loan debt.

I will say that we see massive risk to the individuals. You know, as I said before, 40 percent of the people who enter college aren't graduating. So there's no doubt that the outcomes if you go to college and graduate are better than the outcomes if you don't, but sadly the outcomes if you go to college and don't graduate aren't meaningfully better than the outcomes if you don't go to college at all, and   except that we now have no better outcomes and a lot of debt. And so we really worry about the debt more on an individual basis than we do even on a collective-economy basis. So, of course, we're paying attention to it on a collective-economy basis.

Pro Private Markets
Nordic Capital Buys Data Platform Macrobond

By Ed Ballard 
422 words
10 August 2018
16:10
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Nordic Capital bought Swedish economic data platform Macrobond Financial AB as private-equity interest in financial technology continues to surge.

The deal is Nordic's fifth from its  4.3 billion ninth fund, which the firm said closed in May, 23% above its target. The size of the transaction was not disclosed.

The company's founders, including Chief Executive Tomas Liljeborg, are reinvesting alongside Nordic, the person familiar with the matter said.

Macrobond was founded in 2008 in Malm, Sweden, and has since opened offices in London and New York. It runs a platform that lets users access a database of macroeconomic and financial information, analyze data and build charts. Its clients range from banks and institutional investors to universities.

"The demand of financial big data analysis and visualization is increasing continuously and Macrobond's management team has an outstanding proven track record in financial software analytics," said Nordic Capital Partner Fredrik Nslun.

Macrobond fits with two popular themes among private-equity firms. As a software-as-a-service platform, it offers the prospect of stable revenue and loyal customers. Its core offering, financial data, is also in demand among private-equity investors.

In one of the largest-ever private-equity deals, a group led by Blackstone Group LP agreed in January to buy a majority stake in Thomson Reuters Corp.'s data and terminal business for $17 billion.

Smaller data deals this year included Citic Capital Partners' purchase of Euromoney Institutional Investor PLC's market intelligence arm for $180.5 million and Phoenix Equity Partners' 95 million purchase of economic forecaster Capital Economics Ltd.

Nordic Capital has a long history of backing fintech companies. Last year it scored a notable success with the  1.5 billion sale of payments company Bambora AB to French rival Ingenico Group. The exit delivered a five-times return on Nordic's investment, WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported.

The value of private equity-backed European fintech deals is already close to $10 billion this year, compared with $5.2 billion in 2017, according to data provider PitchBook Data Inc. This year's total was boosted by Hellman & Friedman's $5.3 billion take-private of Danish payments firm Nets A/S, which completed in February.

At this rate, 2018 may be the biggest year for buyout firms investing in European fintech, surpassing 2014 when total deal value hit $13 billion, according to PitchBook.

Write to Ed Ballard at "mailto:ed.ballard@wsj.com"

Pro Private Markets
WSJ Pro Private Equity's Summer Reading List

By the WSJ Pro Private Equity Editorial Team 
1,352 words
10 August 2018
11:30
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Nearly a decade ago, we decided to ask professionals from across the private-equity and venture-capital industries what they were reading, and our first annual summer reading list was born. This year's contributors include limited partners and general partners, both from long-established firms and newer ones. Although their individual preferences vary, their contributions reflect a shared intellectual curiosity and love of reading.

Christopher Ailman, chief investment officer, California State Teachers' Retirement System

"The Intelligent Investor: The Definitive Book on Value Investing" by Benjamin Graham

"As a primer for the beginning investor, it's an oldie, but a goodie."

"Misbehaving: The Making of Behavioral Economics" by Richard H. Thaler

"A fun read for economics nerds like me."

Beth Ferreira, managing director, FirstMark Capital

"3 Kings: Diddy, Dr. Dre, Jay-Z, and Hip-Hop's Multibillion-Dollar Rise" by Zack O'Malley Greenburg

"I'm reading this book because I am fascinated by opportunities in cultural movements. In this case, what created the opportunity to develop large lasting lifestyle brands like Beats by Dre or Diddy's Croc vodka."

Britt Harris, chief executive, University of Texas Investment Management Co.

"Predicting the Markets: A Professional Autobiography" by Edward Yardeni

"Traversing the financial markets is a dangerous and often treacherous undertaking. It can also be rewarding. We need a guide who has traveled the terrain over many years and under all kinds of unusual conditions. Ed Yardeni is that guide for America, and this book lays out a vast array of information that will be helpful for every investor who hopes to make their journey a success."

Pam Hendrickson, chief operating officer and vice chairman, Riverside Co.

"Just Mercy: A Story of Justice and Redemption" by Bryan Stevenson

"It's a memoir by a world class lawyer who defends people on death row! We are all implicated when we allow others to be mistreated. A story about the importance of mercy and compassion."

Julia Kahr, senior managing director, Blackstone Group LP

"Pachinko" by Min Jin Lee

"I read modern fiction as a way to relax and decompress.  Pachinko' captured my imagination not only because it is a lovely family saga crossing multiple generations in Japan and Korea, but also because the theme of insularity between these two countries is relevant to much of the geopolitical discourse today."

Andrew Kuper, chief executive, LeapFrog Investments

"Genghis Khan and the Making of the Modern World" by Jack Weatherford

"This revisionist history tackles many Western myths about the history of societies and governance in the East. Genghis and his successors, far from overseeing brutal hordes, were enlightened innovators in both economics and governance. Some of the book's arguments are overblown or inaccurate, but overall it leads us to rethink the foundations of the modern world, especially Asia and other emerging markets, in which we invest."

Kyle Lefkoff, general partner, Boulder Ventures

"How to Change Your Mind: What the New Science of Psychedelics Teaches Us About Consciousness, Dying, Addiction, Depression, and Transcendence" by Michael Pollan

"The medical history of psychedelic drugs and their resurgence as a treatment for depression and addiction. I've loved Pollan's books about food in the past, which combine his first-person accounts with solid history and context. This one was great, too, and should spark an important public discussion around the proper use of LSD and psilocybin in the modern practice of psychiatry."

Adam Levin, partner, Bain Capital Ventures

"The Old Man and the Sea" by Ernest Hemingway

"I am heading to Cuba over Labor Day weekend so decided to start rereading  The Old Man and the Sea.' The last time I read it, I was in seventh grade. I always try to read a work of fiction set in my future travel destination before a trip. Hemingway illustrates the essence of the country through this sense of magical realism, as he details an elderly Cuban fisherman &out at sea looking for a giant marlin. The language is simple, yet the story hits on the complex themes of triumph and loss."

Roger McNamee, co-founder, Elevation Partners

"Antisocial Media: How Facebook Disconnects Us and Undermines Democracy" by Siva Vaidhyanathan

"The country has entrusted the integrity of the 2018 midterm elections to Facebook, along with Twitter and Google. Is that a good idea or total insanity? Professor Siva Vaidhyanathan of the University of Virginia has studied the issue and shares his perspective in a brilliant book."

Mike Miles, advisory director, Berkshire Partners

"The Bitter Woods: The Battle of the Bulge" by John S.D. Eisenhower

"Although it gets pretty deep into the detail of troop units and positions, it gives a very readable and credible (the author was Ike's son) account of one of the most important battles Americans have ever fought. It also covers how pride and what seem like petty jealousies almost sidetracked the British-American alliance. If those issues can impact great leaders like Eisenhower, Bradley and Montgomery in the midst of the most important enterprise of the 20th century, it should not surprise us that they crop up in other organizations all the time."

Tricia Patrick, managing director, Advent International

"Sapiens: A Brief History of Humankind" by Yuval Noah Harari, "Why We Can't Wait" by Martin Luther King Jr. and "The Banker's Wife" by Cristina Alger ("Who was in my Goldman Sachs analyst class and is now a highly published author")

"Nonfiction is wonderful for keeping the mind enriched, but getting lost in great fiction is almost meditative."

Jackie Rantanen, managing director, Hamilton Lane

"The Ambition Decisions: What Women Know About Work, Family, and the Path to Building a Life" by Hana Schank and Elizabeth Wallace

"I'm intrigued by this profile of a group of female classmates 20 years after they graduated from Northwestern, and the diversity of life and career choices they've made along the way. It speaks to the evolving definitions of success for women."

Gary Sernovitz, managing director, Lime Rock Partners

"Bad Blood: Secrets and Lies in a Silicon Valley Startup" by John Carreyrou

"When reading  Bad Blood,' the story of the Theranos blood-testing startup fraud by The Wall Street Journal investigate reporter who unmasked it, I alternated between two conclusions. One was that the fraud, which resulted in a $9 billion valuation at its peak, was so wild, odd, brazen, and sociopathic that there is nothing of practical use to professional investors. The second conclusion, though, was that Theranos is a case study in why we need professional, industry-specialist investors to separate optimism from hype and to not allocate capital to bad ideas and bad people. But whether  Bad Blood' is practical or not, the book should qualify for your summer reading list for the most basic reason: It is a fun, fast, detailed, alive and surprising read that's hard to put down."

Dave Tamburri, managing partner, Health Enterprise Partners

"Hit Makers: How to Succeed in an Age of Distraction" by Derek Thompson

"The way we shape our message to the market about a product or service is based on science and art. In  Hit Makers,' Thompson uses a variety of examples to highlight the  customer paradox' consumers innately want something both partly new as well as somewhat familiar."

Brendan Wallace, managing partner, Fifth Wall Ventures

"Life 3.0: Being Human in the Age of Artificial Intelligence" by Max Tegmark

"A remarkably accessible book that delves into the complex social, economic and ethical implications of superhuman AI. Most of us are familiar with Hollywood-esque, dystopian depictions of AI as  Terminator' robots indiscriminately killing humans. However, to Tegmark, evil robots are a theatrical distraction from the real risk: a general intelligence that exceeds our own. Life 3.0's focus is on the nature of intelligence itself and why general intelligence, and perhaps even consciousness, need not be confined to the neurons and tissue of the human brain."

This column appeared in the August 2018 issue of Private Equity Analyst, a monthly magazine published by WSJ Pro.
