Carrick Capital Partners is ramping up its investment in Discovery Health Partners, nearly three years after the firm initially backed the company.

The growth investment firm has purchased a majority stake in Discovery, betting the provider of cloud-based services to health plans and other health-care organizations will continue to expand its reach.

Based in Itasca, Ill., Discovery offers a platform that helps clients such as health insurers manage their payments and revenue-collecting processes. That includes ensuring that payment requests are legitimate and that insurers are compensated for the services they provide, particularly by government-sponsored programs such as Medicare, said Steve Unterberger, a managing director at Carrick.

Carrick "https://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120150818eb8iqg8r7?mod=undefined" Discovery in 2015, investing $22.5 million in its parent company, LaunchPoint Ventures LLC. Discovery today has more than 70 customers in the U.S., including regional and national health-care plans, Mr. Unterberger said.

Discovery in January named Jason Brown as its new chief executive. The company is working on new business tools based on data analytics and machine learning that it expects will help add new customers, Mr. Unterberger noted.

Carrick invests in companies that use technology to make business processes more efficient, focusing on three areas: software as a service, business process outsourcing and transaction processing. The firm, which has offices in San Francisco and Newport Beach, Calif., typically targets businesses with annual revenue of $20 million to $100 million and positive earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, according to its website.

The firm recently invested $80 million to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/carrick-capital-invests-80-million-to-take-a-minority-stake-in-exiger-1531159496?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1" in Exiger LLC, a provider of regulatory, financial-crime, risk and compliance consulting services and technology. The deal was one of the largest in Carrick's history, Mr. Unterberger said.

Carrick's other portfolio companies include "https://www.wsj.com/articles/carrick-backs-perfect-sense-with-22-million-investment-1484312400?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=4", a software maker that helps businesses produce digital content; and Saviynt Inc., a provider of cloud-security and identity-governance services.

The company said on Thursday that it had met its leverage target, which would allow it to  move towards a policy of increasing distributions to shareholders over time. 

Hastings also joined the growing chorus of insurance companies complaining about rising costs.

 We have 	. 	. 	. 	seen adverse weather, continued fraud activity across the market, and some increase in the cost of repairs and mid-range bodily injury claims, resulting in a claims inflation outlook for the full year towards the upper end of a 3 per cent to 5 per cent range,  said chief executive Toby van der Meer.

Although Hastings managed to push up its prices by 2 per cent in the first half of the year, premiums more generally have been falling. Claims inflation could reverse that trend, however.

Pro Private Markets
The Roundup

6,453 words
8 August 2018
16:20
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

U.S. & CANADA

BUYOUTS

AE Industrial Partners, Boca Raton, Fla.

AE Industrial Partners raised $1.36 billion for its second private-equity fund. The firm surpassed a $1 billion target for AE Industrial Partners Fund II LP, which is larger than the $680 million debut fund the firm closed in 2016. Investors that disclosed commitments to the new fund include the Baltimore City Fire & Police Employees' Retirement System, Los Angeles County Employees Retirement Association and New Mexico Educational Retirement Board. AE Industrial targets controlling investments in industrial, aerospace, defense and power-generation companies.

Alliance Consumer Growth, New York

Growth-equity firm Alliance Consumer Growth closed its fourth fund, Alliance Consumer Growth Fund IV LP, at the vehicle's $350 million hard cap. Alliance provides minority capital to consumer-product and retail brands. The firm typically invests $5 million to $25 million per deal. Alliance targets companies with revenue of at least $5 million and can back businesses with revenue of more than $50 million. The firm closed on $210 million in capital commitments for its third fund in 2016.

Beekman Group, New York

Beekman Group closed its fourth fund at $425 million, reaching the vehicle's hard cap and exceeding a $375 million target. The final tally for Beekman Investment Partners IV LP is nearly double the size of the firm's previous fund. Beekman disclosed a final closing for that pool of capital at $230 million in 2015. The firm typically invests in companies that have enterprise values of $15 million to $200 million and provide services in the consumer, health-care, business and industrial sectors.

Black Bay Energy Capital, New Orleans

Black Bay Energy Capital held a final closing for its inaugural fund, Black Bay Energy LP, with commitments of $224 million, exceeding a $200 million target. The firm focuses on investments requiring up to $30 million of equity in North American oil-field services and equipment companies. Black Bay is backed by investors that include endowments, foundations, private pensions, funds of funds and family offices.

Blackstone Group LP, New York

Blackstone Group LP collected at least $2.77 billion so far for a tactical opportunities fund, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The Teachers' Retirement System of Louisiana is one limited partners that committed to Blackstone Tactical Opportunities Fund III LP. Blackstone's tactical opportunities group takes a flexible approach, investing across asset classes, industries and geographies. The fund's predecessor collected at least $3.72 billion, according to a March 2016 SEC filing, though it isn't clear how much it ultimately gathered.

Cornell Capital, New York

Cornell Capital, an investment firm established by former Goldman Sachs Group Inc. executive Henry Cornell, closed its first multiasset private-equity fund with nearly $1.33 billion in commitments. The final closing of Cornell Capital Partners LP brings the firm's total assets to more than $2.2 billion. The fund had a $2 billion offering amount, according to an April 2017 SEC filing. Cornell Capital invests in the consumer, energy, financial and industrial sectors.

Denham Capital, Boston

Denham Capital closed its first stand-alone mining vehicle at $558 million to expand its portfolio in Australia, Canada, South America and Africa. The fund, Denham Mining Fund LP, targets investments in base metals, metallurgical coal, and industrial and specialty minerals. Founded in 2004, Denham is an energy- and resources-focused firm with more than $9.8 billion in invested and committed capital.

Five States Energy Capital, Dallas

Five States Energy Capital is planning to raise up to $150 million this year to buy mature oil-and-gas properties in the U.S. The firm will seek $100 million to $150 million for a new fund, having recently finished deploying Energy Capital Fund 2 LLC, which secured $112 million in 2014, said Arthur Budge Jr., the firm's president and chief executive. Five States concentrates on acquiring mature, conventional onshore oil-and-gas fields that it usually holds for many years, Mr. Budge said. The firm also provides mezzanine capital to oil-and-gas companies and invests in midstream projects.

Health Enterprise Partners, New York

Health Enterprise Partners rounded up $188 million for a vehicle aimed at expanding health-care services and health-care information-technology companies. Health Enterprise began fundraising for Health Enterprise Partners III LP with a $150 million target, said Managing Partner Dave Tamburri. The new fund tops the firm's second fund, which was raised in 2012 and closed on $148 million. The firm makes growth-equity and buyout investments.

Insight Venture Partners, New York

Technology investor Insight Venture Partners collected $6.3 billion for Insight Venture Partners X LP. The software-focused firm backs everything from startups to larger public-to-private deals. Unlike some firms that have separate funds for venture-capital, growth and buyout deals, Insight has opted to invest in all types of technology and software deals from a single fund. The Los Angeles Fire & Police Pensions is one of the fund's backers. Insight closed its previous fund in the series at $3.29 billion alongside a $1.46 billion co-investment vehicle in 2015.

J.W. Childs Associates, Waltham, Mass.

J.W. Childs Associates held a first closing and gathered about $250 million in commitments for J.W. Childs Equity Partners V LP, said a person familiar with the fundraising process. J.W. Childs last year listed a $500 million goal for the fund in a SEC filing. The first closing is evidence of a continuing post-financial-crisis rebound by the firm. The new vehicle is the second full-fledged fund raised by J.W. Childs since 2002. Its predecessor vehicle was formed in 2014 with $358 million, WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported. Four years ago, J.W. Childs organized a recapitalization of JW Childs Equity Partners III LP, a 2002-vintage, $1.75 billion vehicle. The firm invests in the consumer-product, retail and health care-services sectors.

Luminate Capital Partners, San Francisco

Luminate Capital Partners is expected to close its sophomore fund at $425 million, said people familiar with the matter. The firm focuses on majority investments in software and software-based services companies. Luminate's first fund, Luminate Capital Partners LP, closed on more than $265 million last year.

Macquarie Group Ltd., Sydney

The infrastructure asset-management arm of Australian firm Macquarie Group Ltd. raised at least $3.84 billion so far for Macquarie Infrastructure Partners IV LP and a related vehicle, according to a SEC filing. The pool of capital follows Macquarie Infrastructure Partners III LP, a North American infrastructure fund that reached its $3 billion hard cap in 2014.

Manulife Financial Corp., Toronto

Canadian insurer Manulife Financial Corp. made its first move into managing private infrastructure bets for other investors with a roughly $2 billion fund focused on assets such as utilities, transmission lines and transportation systems in the U.S. But Manulife ultimately will have up to a total of $4 billion in joint infrastructure investments. The insurer transferred stakes in some of its own past investments into the fund, John Hancock Infrastructure Fund LP, and is matching every dollar of new deals with money from its general account. French private-equity firm Ardian led a group of outside investors that pledged about $1 billion to the new offering, said people familiar with the matter.

Palladium Equity Partners, New York

Palladium Equity Partners collected at least $819.9 million so far for Palladium Equity Partners V LP, according to a SEC filing. If the fund hits a $1.5 billion goal listed in the filing, it would beat the $1.14 billion Palladium reached for Palladium Equity Partners IV LP in 2014. One backer of the new vehicle is the Oregon State Treasury, according to pension fund documents. Palladium targets investments in lower midmarket companies it believes will benefit from growth in the U.S. Hispanic population.

PPC Partners, Chicago

PPC Partners closed PPC Fund II LP at the vehicle's $1.8 billion hard cap. The fund exceeded an initial $1.5 billion target. PPC, recently formed by Pritzker Group Private Capital, acquires and operates North American midmarket companies in the manufactured-products, services and health-care sectors.

Providence Equity Partners, Providence, R.I.

Providence Equity Partners' growth-equity arm wrapped up its third fund with $1.3 billion in commitments. The fund, Providence Strategic Growth III LP, reached its hard cap and collected more than double the $640 million Providence Strategic Growth Capital Partners gathered for its second fund, which closed in 2016. One pension that disclosed a commitment to the fund is the Texas Municipal Retirement System, according to a public document. Providence Strategic Growth primarily invests in lower midmarket software and technology-enabled services companies in North America.

Raine Group, New York

Raine Group, a merchant bank focused on technology, media and telecommunications, collected at least $579 million so far for a new fund, according to a SEC filing. The amount recorded for Raine Partners III LP is more than half of the $850 million the firm disclosed for its second growth-equity fund in early 2015. One limited partner that disclosed a commitment to the new fund is the Tennessee Consolidated Retirement System.

Revelstoke Capital Partners, Denver

Revelstoke Capital Partners collected at least $302.4 million so far for Revelstoke Capital Partners Fund II LP, according to a SEC filing. Two people familiar with the matter said Revelstoke is targeting $500 million for the offering. Revelstoke's debut pool of capital closed with $307 million in 2015. The firm invests in health-care and business-services companies.

Silver Creek Capital Management, Seattle

Silver Creek Capital Management is seeking $1 billion for a fund that will buy reserves of sand, gravel and other construction materials from suppliers in exchange for royalty payments. Silver Creek plans to use money from the investment vehicle, Silver Creek Aggregate Reserves Fund I, to acquire stores of construction materials from suppliers, said a person familiar with the matter. Those suppliers then will enter into long-term agreements with Silver Creek and pay royalties to the vehicle based on their sales of finished products.

Starwood Energy Group Global, Greenwich, Conn.

Starwood Energy Group Global closed its third energy infrastructure fund with more than $1.2 billion in capital commitments. Starwood Energy Infrastructure Fund III raised more than its predecessor. The firm disclosed a final closing for that vehicle at $983 million in early 2014. The Oregon Public Employees Retirement System is a backer of the new vehicle, according to a pension fund document.

Stonepeak Infrastructure Partners, New York

Stonepeak Infrastructure Partners raised $7.2 billion for Stonepeak Infrastructure Fund III LP. The North American midmarket infrastructure investor surpassed a $5 billion target for the vehicle and closed the fund at its hard cap. Stonepeak disclosed a $3.5 billion closing for its second fund in early 2016.

Sun Capital Partners, Boca Raton, Fla.

Sun Capital Partners is seeking $2.2 billion for Sun Capital Partners VII LP, according to a SEC filing. The firm didn't record any commitments to the fund in the filing. Sun Capital emphasizes investments in the consumer and industrial sectors, but also invests in areas such as defense, business services, energy, health care and technology. The firm closed its prior fund at $2.1 billion in 2014, short of an initial $3 billion target, WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported.

Thompson Street Capital Partners, St. Louis

Midmarket investor Thompson Street Capital Partners closed its fifth fund at $1.15 billion. The vehicle, Thompson Street Capital Partners V LP, wrapped up at its hard cap and exceeded an $850 million target. The fund size is a step up from Thompson Street's fourth fund, which closed on $640 million in 2015. Thompson Street invests in services-oriented health-care, technology and software companies, as well as in business-services and engineered-products companies. One institution that disclosed a commitment to the fund is the Oklahoma Police Pension and Retirement System.

TowerBrook Capital Partners, New York

TowerBrook Capital Partners, the private-equity shop that spun out of George Soros's investment firm, raised $5.3 billion for its flagship buyout fund and another vehicle focused on minority stakes. The firm gathered $4.25 billion for TowerBrook V and $1.05 billion for TowerBrook Structured Opportunities II. The Massachusetts Pension Reserves Investment Management Board disclosed a commitment to the fund. TowerBrook typically uses its private-equity funds to take majority stakes in large and midsize companies throughout the U.S. and Europe. The firm invests its structured opportunities funds primarily in noncontrol stressed and distressed debt, structured equity and structured assets, according to TowerBrook's latest registered investment adviser filing.

Valor Equity Partners, Chicago

Growth-focused Valor Equity Partners held a final closing for a new fund at $1.05 billion. WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported the firm had set a $900 million hard cap for Valor Equity Partners IV LP but later increased that amount. The Illinois Municipal Retirement Fund is a backer of the vehicle. Fund IV is expected to take controlling and noncontrolling stakes in companies. Valor's third fund closed with $490 million in 2015.

Welsh Carson Anderson & Stowe, New York

Welsh Carson Anderson & Stowe is back on the fundraising trail, said people familiar with the matter. The firm is marketing Welsh Carson Anderson & Stowe XIII to potential limited partners and has set a $4 billion hard cap, said one of the people. The Minnesota State Board of Investment committed up to $250 million to the vehicle, said Mansco Perry, executive director at Minnesota State. Welsh Carson primarily invests in U.S. health-care and technology businesses, according to a March registered investment adviser filing. The firm's previous pool of capital closed with $3.33 billion in 2016, according to SEC filings.

DISTRESSED/TURNAROUND

Aterian Investment Partners, New York

Aterian Investment Partners wrapped up fundraising for Aterian Investment Partners III LP with more than $350 million. The firm invests in businesses that are underperforming, distressed or require turnarounds. Aterian also invests in noncore assets of businesses and pursues other investment situations it considers unique. The new fund's predecessor closed on $256.8 million in 2013.

Lone Star Funds, Dallas

Lone Star Funds is back on the fundraising trail seeking $6 billion for a new vehicle, according to a SEC filing. Lone Star didn't record any commitments to Lone Star Fund XI LP in the filing. The fund is a follow-up to a pool of capital the firm closed in 2016 with about $5.6 billion. That pool targets deals in North America, Europe, Latin America and the Asia-Pacific region, including investments in operating companies and residential, corporate and consumer debt. Lone Star seeks to invest in markets that have suffered economic or banking crises.

Platinum Equity, Beverly Hills, Calif.

Platinum Equity closed its maiden small-cap fund at the vehicle's $1.5 billion hard cap. Although Platinum primarily invests in the U.S., the firm's Platinum Equity Small Cap Fund LP also considers deals in Europe, according to a February document published by the Pennsylvania Public School Employees' Retirement System, which committed to the fund. The document stated the pool of capital would seek to capture a slice of the underperforming businesses that generate less than $450 million of revenue and less than $45 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization throughout North America and Europe.

Waterous Energy Fund, Calgary, Alberta

Waterous Energy Fund raised about 1.4 billion Canadian dollars ($1.05 billion) to invest in North American oil-and-gas companies. The firm closed its first fund, Waterous Energy Fund LP, above an initial C$1 billion target and at the vehicle's hard cap. Waterous targets businesses in special situations that typically require recapitalizations, restructurings or other types of repositioning. The firm looks to invest C$300 million to C$500 million of equity capital per deal. Waterous has invested more than 60% of the fund in Strath Resources Ltd. and Cona Resources Ltd., two oil-and-gas companies operating in Canada.

FUNDS OF FUNDS

Diversified Trust, Nashville, Tenn.

Wealth management firm Diversified Trust closed its sixth fund family with $155.6 million, including the general partner commitment. The final closing of DTC Private Equity VI LP and DTC Private Equity VI-Q LP came in ahead of a $150 million goal. The firm plans to commit money from the fund to a portfolio of 20 to 25 private-equity and venture-capital fund managers.

Fort Washington Capital Partners Group, Cincinnati

Fort Washington Capital Partners Group, the private-equity arm of Fort Washington Investment Advisors Inc., is seeking $500 million for its newest fund-of-funds offering, according to SEC filings. The total offering amount for Fort Washington Private Equity Investors X LP and its related parallel vehicles is larger than the $350 million the firm secured for the fund's predecessor in 2017. The Teachers' Retirement System of the State of Kentucky committed to the new fund.

Industry Ventures, San Francisco

Industry Ventures closed a new fund of funds with $335 million. The firm will invest the vehicle, Industry Ventures Partnership Holdings V LP, in early-stage venture-capital funds through primary commitments and secondary purchases, as well as making direct co-investments alongside those funds. The fund of funds is the successor to a vehicle the firm closed in 2016. Industry Ventures wrapped up that vehicle and a debut direct co-investment fund together with more than $400 million.

MEZZANINE & OTHER DEBT

Angel Island Capital, San Francisco

Angel Island Capital, a specialty finance company owned by Golden Gate Capital, is raising its first fund from outside backers. Angel Island is looking to raise $750 million for AIC Credit Opportunity Partners Fund II LP, according to a presentation for potential investors reviewed by WSJ Pro Private Equity. The firm invests in midmarket companies' non-investment-grade debt. The fund will back services, industrials, software and technology companies in North America, according to the investor materials.

Clearview Capital, Stamford, Conn.

Clearview Capital held a first and final closing for two new funds, which collectively raised $658 million. The firm's latest midmarket private-equity fund, Clearview Capital Fund IV LP, closed on its $550 million hard cap. Clearview also collected $108 million for Clearview Capital Mezzanine Fund I LP. The firm seeks to invest in business-services, health-care, services, manufacturing and specialized distribution companies. Clearview closed its third private-equity fund in 2013 with $325 million.

SECONDARY

Second Alpha Partners, New York

Second Alpha Partners closed its latest investment fund, Second Alpha Partners IV LP, with $75.6 million in commitments. The fund's portfolio includes technology companies OpenX Software Ltd., Code42 Software Inc. and ON24 Inc. Second Alpha is focused on secondary and secondary-linked direct investments in mature venture stage, growth-equity and midmarket companies. The firm targets deals in the technology, media, telecommunications and technology-enabled services sectors.

Whitehorse Liquidity Partners, Toronto

Whitehorse Liquidity Partners, an investment firm that specializes in restructuring private-equity portfolios through preferred-equity deals, raised at least $975.4 million so far for Whitehorse Liquidity Partners II LP, according to a SEC filing. The firm is seeking to raise $1 billion for the fund, according to the filing. Whitehorse closed its debut fund at $400 million in 2017.

VENTURE CAPITAL

Andreessen Horowitz, Menlo Park, Calif.

Andreessen Horowitz closed on $300 million for a new blockchain-focused fund. The fund, called a16z crypto, was designed so that it wouldn't be subject to the 20% cap on cryptocurrency investments that standard venture-capital funds face. The vehicle is the first fund a mainstream venture firm has dedicated to cryptocurrency and blockchain startups.

Armory Square Ventures, New York

Armory Square Ventures, an early-stage investor focused on upstate New York, raised at least $18.5 million so far for its second fund, according to a SEC filing. It isn't clear whether the firm has held a final closing for Armory Square Ventures II LP.

Builders VC, San Francisco

Builders VC raised at least $115.5 million so far for Builders VC Fund I LP, which has a $200 million goal, according to a SEC filing. The firm also recently closed on at least $10 million for a separate fund, Builders VC Entrepreneurs Fund I LP, according to a separate SEC filing. It isn't known whether that fund has officially wrapped up.

Bullpen Capital, San Francisco

Bullpen Capital closed a $138 million fund to address companies seeking to raise "supersize" Series A rounds. The fund, Bullpen Capital IV LP, initially targeted $125 million. General Partner Paul Martino said the firm won't change its investment strategy, but it will have more capital to invest in companies' follow-on rounds. In addition to its fourth main fund, Bullpen is raising a new opportunity fund dubbed the Playoff fund that will enable it to make follow-on investments from the firm's first three funds. It will be about $40 million, and likely will close in the summer or early fall. Bullpen closed its third fund with $75 million in 2016.

Cayuga Venture Fund, Ithaca, N.Y.

Cayuga Venture Fund is targeting as much as $100 million for its newest fund focused on early-stage investments in New York. If Cayuga Venture Fund VI LP hits that target, the fund would be larger than its predecessor. Cayuga's previous fund raised a total of $37 million from 2015 to 2017, said Managing Partner Zachary Shulman. The firm targets investments in upstate New York and New York City.

Crosslink Capital, San Francisco

Crosslink Capital raised $275 million for its latest early-stage venture fund. Crosslink Ventures VIII is a step up from the firm's seventh venture-capital fund, which closed on $170 million in 2015. The larger fund will provide "more capital to heavy up on our winners," said Eric Chin, a partner at the firm.

Felicis Ventures, Menlo Park, Calif.

Felicis Ventures started raising its next fund with a $240 million target, said people familiar with the situation. The firm primarily focuses on early-stage startups in the mobile, e-commerce, enterprise, education and health sectors. The firm closed its fifth fund with $200 million in 2016.

Fifth Wall Ventures, Venice, Calif.

Fifth Wall Ventures raised at least $100.8 million so far for a new fund, according to a SEC filing. The firm is seeking $400 million for Fifth Wall Ventures II LP, according to the filing. Fifth Wall targets investments in real estate-technology businesses. The firm's limited partners include real-estate companies such as CBRE Group Inc. and Equity Residential. Fifth Wall closed its first fund with $212 million in 2017.

Glasswing Ventures, Boston

Glasswing Ventures closed a $112 million fund focused on artificial-intelligence startups on the East Coast. The firm set a $150 million goal for Glasswing Ventures LP in a SEC filing about two years ago. Managing Partner Rudina Seseri said the firm's goal had been to raise more than $100 million. Glasswing will invest in both seed and preseed companies making cybersecurity and enterprise software.

Greycroft Partners, New York

Greycroft Partners collected $256 million for its fifth early-stage fund. The vehicle is a step up from the firm's previous fund, which closed with $200 million in 2015. Greycroft backs consumer- and business-facing startups. The firm's partners contributed $6 million to Greycroft V, said Ian Sigalow, a co-founder and partner of the firm. Greycroft now has $1.3 billion under management.

High Alpha, Indianapolis

Startup studio High Alpha raised more than $100 million for two funds. High Alpha Capital Fund II LP closed with $85 million to make seed and Series A investments in enterprise-software startups. High Alpha Studio II LLC secured $16.7 million from Emergence Capital and Foundry Group to invest in enterprise-cloud companies. The firm's previous High Alpha Capital fund collected $21 million.

Impact America, Oakland, Calif.

Impact America is seeking $50 million for a new pool of capital, according to a SEC filing. The early-stage firm didn't record any commitments to Impact America Fund II LP in the filing. Impact America aims to invest in technology-enabled businesses that benefit low-to-moderate income communities in the U.S.

MetaProp NYC, New York

MetaProp NYC closed its second early-stage real-estate technology fund at $40 million. Real-estate-services firm Cushman & Wakefield PLC is a limited partner in the vehicle. MetaProp Ventures II LP will invest $150,000 to $2 million in up to 40 real-estate technology providers.

Osage Partners, Bala Cynwyd, Pa.

Osage Partners set out to raise what would be its largest fund yet for investing in technology emerging from universities and research institutions. The firm is seeking $250 million for Osage University Partners III LP, according to a SEC filing. If it hits that goal, the fund would surpass the $215 million Osage Partners secured in 2015 for its previous fund in the series.

Owl Ventures, San Francisco

Education-technology investor Owl Ventures is raising a new investment fund with a $225 million goal, according to a SEC filing. The firm didn't record any commitments to Owl Ventures III LP in the filing. Owl closed its second fund at $185 million in 2017.

Rain Capital, San Francisco

Rain Capital raised $10 million toward a $20 million goal for a cybersecurity-focused fund. The firm backs early-stage cybersecurity startups in Silicon Valley. General Partner Chenxi Wang plans to use the fund to promote female entrepreneurs in the cybersecurity sector.

Redpoint Ventures, Menlo Park, Calif.

Redpoint Ventures closed Redpoint Ventures VII LP at $400 million. The fund is the same size as several of the firm's previous vehicles. Its predecessor wrapped up in 2015 to invest in seed, Series A and Series B rounds of consumer and enterprise startups.

Scale Venture Partners, Foster City, Calif.

Scale Venture Partners raised $400 million for its sixth fund. The firm, which closed its previous fund with $335 million in 2016, plans to use the new capital to continue investing in enterprise-software businesses.

Sound Ventures, Beverly Hills, Calif.

Sound Ventures, the firm co-founded by Ashton Kutcher and Guy Oseary, is in the market to raise $150 million for Sound Ventures II LP, according to a SEC filing. The firm didn't record any commitments to the fund in the filing.

SRI Capital, Philadelphia

SRI Capital launched a $100 million early-stage fund for technology startups in the U.S. and India. The fund will invest $1 million to $3 million per deal. SRI expects to make six to eight investments annually. The fund was anchored by a $20 million commitment from the family office of managing partner Sashi Reddi.

TCV, Menlo Park, Calif.

TCV is seeking $2.5 billion for TCV X LP, according to an investor document reviewed by WSJ Pro Private Equity. The firm backs growth-stage public and private technology companies, making minority and majority investments. With the fund, the firm plans to make 25 to 30 investments of $75 million to $150 million each. TCV closed its previous fund with $2.5 billion in 2016.

Telescope Partners, San Francisco

Former Sequoia Capital partner Mickey Arabelovic raised at least $79.1 million so far for Telescope Partners I LP, according to a SEC filing. Mr. Arabelovic's growth-equity firm, Telescope Partners, surpassed a $75 million goal initially recorded for the fund in a 2016 filing. It isn't known if the fund has held an official closing. The firm also recorded $1.2 million for Telescope Partners SP I LP in a separate filing.

Urban Innovation Fund, San Francisco

Urban Innovation Fund closed its first fund with $22.5 million. The seed fund, Urban Innovation Fund I LP, focuses on startups that address urban problems. One of Urban Innovation's recent deals is an investment in Voatz Inc., which is developing a blockchain-based mobile voting technology.

Westly Group, Menlo Park, Calif.

Westly Group raised $130 million toward its third fund, which will focus on clean energy and mobility. The firm manages more than $400 million across its three funds. Westly typically invests $3 million to $5 million per deal.

EUROPE

BUYOUTS

Apiary Capital, London

Apiary Capital, a firm set up by former Bowmark Capital and Inflexion Private Equity executives, hit the 200 million ($265.7 million) hard cap for its first fund, beating an initial 150 million target, said Mark Salter, the lower midmarket firm's managing partner. Apiary will seek to make eight to 12 platform investments in total, primarily targeting the business-services sector but also investing in health-care and education companies, Mr. Salter said.

Bridges Fund Management, London

Impact investor Bridges Fund Management raised 50 million ($66.3 million) for a permanent-capital fund named Bridges Evergreen Holdings. The firm, which hit its target for the fund, aims to back health-care and education businesses that are helping to tackle social and environmental challenges. The fund is structured as a holding company rather than a fund so that it can hold assets over the long term. The vehicle targets profitable small and midsize companies.

Cabot Square Capital, London

Cabot Square Capital plans to raise its sixth buyout fund next year. The firm expects to return to market in the first quarter of 2019, it said in a statement. Cabot Square, which closed its fifth fund in 2016 at 304.5 million, will target around the same amount for the new pool, a spokeswoman said. Cabot Square aims to make control investments in the financial-services, infrastructure and property sectors in the U.K. and Western Europe.

Cathay Capital Private Equity, Paris

Cathay Capital Private Equity held a first closing for its second midmarket fund at  600 million ($701 million). The fund primarily will invest in Chinese and European companies that are expanding internationally. Cathay is targeting  1.2 billion for Cathay Midcap II and expects to close the vehicle in the first half of 2019. The firm's first midcap vehicle closed at  500 million in 2014. Cathay secured investment for the new fund from state-owned banks Bpifrance and China Development Bank. The firm said its fund is the first to receive investment from the China-EU Coinvestment Fund.

ECI Partners, London

Midmarket buyout firm ECI Partners raised 700 million ($930 million) for its 11th fund. ECI 11 received backing from 25 institutional investors from the U.K., U.S., Europe and Asia. The firm will keep its focus on growth-stage buyouts, backing companies valued at 20 million to 150 million. ECI plans to target the technology, media and telecommunications; business- and financial-services; and consumer sectors. The fund's predecessor closed on 500 million in 2014.

Exponent Private Equity, London

Exponent Private Equity reduced its fundraising target for a new pool of capital, said a person familiar with the matter. The midmarket firm originally told investors it was seeking to raise as much as 1.5 billion ($1.98 billion) and had a 1.3 billion target, but it since has cut the target to 1 billion, the person said. If Exponent hits the revised target, the new pool would be equal in size to the firm's third buyout fund, which closed in 2015. When it wrapped up that fund, Exponent said it was targeting investments in U.K.-based businesses with enterprise values of 75 million to 350 million.

Triton, St. Helier, Jersey

Triton held a final closing for its first lower midmarket fund at  448 million ($523.5 million), said a person familiar with the matter. The Northern Europe-focused firm exceeded its initial  350 million target for Triton Smaller Mid-Cap Fund I and raised the fund's hard cap from  400 million in response to investor demand, the person said. Triton's smaller midcap strategy seeks to make equity investments in companies in the German-speaking and Nordic regions. Triton focuses on the industrials sector and also invests in business-services, consumer and health-care companies.

VENTURE CAPITAL

Abingworth, London

Life-sciences investor Abingworth closed a new fund with $315 million. The vehicle, Abingworth Bioventures VII LP, exceeded its $300 million target. The pool of capital had a $400 million hard cap, WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported. The fund's predecessor closed in 2014 with 225 million, or about $375 million at the time. The firm typically invests $15 million to $30 million per company, taking majority or minority positions in mostly U.S. and European businesses.

BlackFin Capital Partners, Paris

BlackFin Capital Partners raised  180 million ($209.5 million) for a financial-technology fund, beating an initial  150 million target. BlackFin Tech Fund 1 will invest in European startups in the financial-tech, insurance-tech and regulatory-tech sectors. Investors in the fund include Bpifrance and Socit Gnrale Insurance.

Dementia Discovery Fund, London

Dementia Discovery Fund raised 250 million ($331.5 million) to back startups developing new medications for dementia. The fund, which focuses on early-stage investing, said AARP committed $60 million to the effort. Managed by SV Health Investors, Dementia Discovery Fund was formed by global drugmakers that include Biogen Inc., Eli Lilly & Co. and GlaxoSmithKline PLC.

Earlybird Venture Capital, Berlin

Earlybird Venture Capital closed its latest early-stage fund at  175 million ($203.6 million). The firm's new "Digital West" fund focuses on digital-technology startups in Europe.

Forbion Capital Partners, Naarden, Netherlands

Forbion Capital Partners held a first closing for its fourth life-sciences fund at  270 million ($316.8 million), exceeding a  250 target. Forbion IV primarily will target initial stakes of 20% to 50% in European companies but also will make a few North American investments.

Highland Europe, London

Growth-stage investor Highland Europe closed a  463 million ($540.5 million) fund. The firm's third fund will continue to invest in European software and internet-enabled businesses, primarily in Germany, the U.K. and France. Highland's previous investment vehicle wrapped up with  332 million in 2015.

Index Ventures, London

Index Ventures raised $1.65 billion for two new funds. The firm closed on $650 million for its ninth venture-capital fund and $1 billion for its fourth growth-equity fund. Index closed on $550 million for its previous venture fund in 2016 and about $700 million for its last growth-equity fund in 2015. Index will prioritize Series A investing from its venture fund.

ASIA

BUYOUTS

Carlyle Group LP, Washington

Carlyle Group LP raised $6.55 billion for a new buyout fund aimed at investing in the Asia-Pacific region. The firm closed Carlyle Asia Partners V LP ahead of a $5 billion target. With the new vehicle, Carlyle is targeting deals in the consumer and retail; financial-services; telecommunications, media and technology; health-care; and industrial sectors. The firm's previous fund in the series closed at $3.9 billion in 2014.

Longreach Group, Hong Kong

Longreach Group is targeting $650 million for Longreach Capital Partners 3 LP and so far has gathered commitments of about $440 million, said a person familiar with the matter. The new fund has exceeded the size of its predecessor, which closed at $400 million in 2012. The Board of Regents of the University of Michigan is one investor that disclosed a commitment to the fund, according to an endowment document. Longreach invests mainly in businesses focused on Japan, and makes selective deals elsewhere in North Asia. The firm targets both controlling and minority investments, and invests in the technology, industrial, financial-services, business-services and consumer sectors.

VENTURE CAPITAL

DST Global, Hong Kong

DST Global is seeking to raise a new main fund and a co-investment fund, according to a pitch deck seen by WSJ Pro Private Equity. The co-investment fund is targeting $1 billion. The proposed size of the main DST Global VI couldn't be determined. For its previous fund, DST Global V, the firm collected $1.85 billion, along with $900 million for co-investments in late 2015. The main fund will take minority stakes by investing $10 million to $125 million in global technology companies with premoney valuations of $500 million or more, according to the investor presentation.

Matrix Partners, Cambridge, Mass.

Matrix Partners gathered a total of at least $1.2 billion so far for two funds, according to SEC filings. The firm raised at least $750 million Matrix Partners China V LP and at least $450 million for Matrix Partners XI LP, a U.S. venture fund. WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported Matrix was seeking to raise $1.5 billion across three funds, including roughly $300 million for its Matrix Partners India III fund. The new China fund is larger than the $500 million China fund Matrix closed in 2016. The firm closed a U.S. fund in 2013 with $450 million.

SHS Gesellschaft fr Beteiligungsmanagement, Tbingen, Germany

SHS Gesellschaft fr Beteiligungsmanagement received commitments of more than  90 million ($104.6 million) toward a fund that is expected to close at  150 million. SHS V will focus on the life-sciences and medical-technology sectors, investing up to  30 million each in 12 to 15 companies, mostly in Germany, Austria, Switzerland, Scandinavia and the Benelux countries. The firm's previous fund closed with  125 million in 2015.

LATIN AMERICA

BUYOUTS

Alta Growth Capital, Mexico City

Alta Growth Capital is seeking $250 million for a new fund. The firm raised at least $85 million so far for Alta Growth Capital Mexico Fund III LP, according to a SEC filing. Alta focuses on growth-equity deals involving Mexican midmarket companies. The firm also pursues buyout opportunities. Alta's previous fund closed with $152 million in 2014, WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported, citing a person familiar with the matter.

FUNDS OF FUNDS

Spectra Investments, So Paulo

Brazilian firm Spectra Investments is planning to raise a new fund of funds. Spectra will seek about $220 million for Spectra Latin America Private Equity IV LP, said the firm's senior partner, Ricardo Kanitz. Spectra expects to hold a first closing for the fund in November, he said. The firm aims to more than double the $96.6 million it raised for its previous fund, which closed in 2017. Spectra has backed several Brazilian private-equity firms through that vehicle, including Angra Partners, Aqua Capital and Vinci Partners, as well as international Latin American investment strategies, such as Rohatyn Group.

This column appeared in the August 2018 issue of Private Equity Analyst, a monthly magazine published by WSJ Pro.

Pro Private Markets
Benhamou Raising $70 Million Fund for Follow-On Deals; Firm has $40 million in committed capital thus far

By Heather Mack 
175 words
8 August 2018
05:36
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Benhamou Global Ventures is raising a new fund of up to $70 million to invest in early-stage companies.

The Palo Alto, Calif.-based firm has thus far secured $40 million of committed capital for BGV Opportunity Fund, the firm's fourth. BGV said the fund is slated for follow-on investments in portfolio companies from the firm's previous two funds.

The firm's first investment out of the latest fund is an existing portfolio company Virtual Instruments Corp., a San Jose-based analytics and logistics software startup.

Founded in 2003 by Eric Benhamou, the former chairman and CEO of 3Com Corp. and Palm Inc., BGV focuses broadly on enterprise technology ranging from cybersecurity and IoT to cloud infrastructure. Portfolio companies include Tagnos Inc., which makes logistics automation software for hospitals, and video content platform provider Sherpa Digital Media Inc.

Facebook Asks Banks for Customer Data

By Emily Glazer, Deepa Seetharaman and AnnaMaria Andriotis 
923 words
7 August 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

A July 26 decline in Facebook Inc. shares shaved more than $119 billion from the company's value. An Aug. 7 Page One article about Facebook's discussions with banks about possibly sharing data incorrectly said that the decline was more than $120 billion.

(WSJ August 15, 2018)

(END)

Facebook Inc. wants your financial data.

The social-media giant has asked large U.S. banks to share detailed financial information about their customers, including card transactions and checking-account balances, as part of an effort to offer new services to users.

Facebook increasingly wants to be a platform where people buy and sell goods and services, besides connecting with friends. The company over the past year asked JPMorgan Chase & Co., Wells Fargo & Co., Citigroup Inc. and U.S. Bancorp to discuss potential offerings it could host for bank customers on Facebook Messenger, people familiar with the matter said.

Facebook has talked about a feature that would show its users their checking-account balances, the people said. It has also pitched fraud alerts, some of the people said.

Data privacy is a sticking point in the banks' conversations with Facebook, said people familiar with the matter. The talks are taking place as Facebook faces several investigations over its ties to political analytics firm Cambridge Analytica, which accessed data on as many 87 million Facebook users without their consent.

One large U.S. bank pulled away from the talks due to privacy concerns, some of the people said.

Facebook has told banks that the additional customer information could be used to offer services that might entice users to spend more time on Messenger, a person familiar with the discussions said. The company is trying to deepen user engagement: Investors shaved more than $120 billion from its market value in one day last month after it said its growth is starting to slow.

Facebook said it wouldn't use the bank data for ad-targeting purposes or share it with third parties.

"We don't use purchase data from banks or credit-card companies for ads," spokeswoman Elisabeth Diana said. "We also don't have special relationships, partnerships or contracts with banks or credit-card companies to use their customers' purchase data for ads."

Facebook shares climbed sharply Monday on the news, rising 4.45%, marking the biggest one-day gain since last month's historic drop.

Banks face pressure to build relationships with big online platforms, which reach billions of users and drive a growing share of commerce. They also are trying to reach more users digitally. Many struggle to gain traction in mobile payments.

Yet banks are hesitant to hand too much control to third-party platforms such as Facebook. They prefer to keep customers on their own websites and apps.

As part of the proposed deals, Facebook asked banks for information about where their users are shopping with their debit and credit cards outside of purchases they make using Facebook Messenger, the people said. Messenger has some 1.3 billion monthly active users, Chief Operating Officer Sheryl Sandberg said on the company's second-quarter earnings call last month.

Alphabet Inc.'s Google and Amazon.com Inc. also have asked banks to share data if they join with them, in order to provide basic banking services on applications such as Google Assistant and Alexa, according to people familiar with the conversations.

"Like many online companies, we routinely talk to financial institutions about how we can improve people's commerce experiences, like enabling better customer service," Facebook's Ms. Diana said. "An essential part of these efforts is keeping people's information safe and secure."

Facebook has taken a harder public line on privacy since the Cambridge Analytica uproar. A product privacy team has announced new features such as "clear history," which would allow users to prevent the service from collecting their off-Facebook browsing details. It also is making efforts to alert users to its privacy settings.

That hasn't assuaged concerns over Facebook's privacy practices. Bank executives are worried about the breadth of information being sought, even if it means their bank might not being available on certain platforms their customers use. Bank customers would need to opt-in to the proposed Facebook services, the company said in a statement Monday.

JPMorgan isn't "sharing our customers' off-platform transaction data with these platforms, and have had to say no to some things as a result," spokeswoman Trish Wexler said.

Banks view mobile commerce as one of their biggest opportunities but are still running behind technology firms such as PayPal Holdings Inc. and Square Inc. Customers have moved slowly, too; many Americans still prefer using credit or debit cards, along with cash and checks.

In an effort to compete with PayPal's Venmo, a group of large banks last year connected their smartphone apps to money-transfer network Zelle. Results are mixed so far: While usage has risen, many banks still aren't on the platform.

In recent years, Facebook has tried to transform Messenger into a hub for customer service and commerce, in keeping with a broader trend among mobile messaging services.

A partnership with American Express Co. allows Facebook users to contact the card company's representatives. Last year, Facebook struck a deal with PayPal that allows users of that payment service to send money through Messenger. And Mastercard Inc. cardholders can place online orders with certain merchants through Messenger using the card company's Masterpass digital wallet. (A Mastercard spokesman said Facebook doesn't see the card users' information.)

High-Speed Adjusting Trips Insurers

By Leslie Scism and Nicole Friedman 
706 words
7 August 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Property insurers are relying on more drones, small aircraft and artificial intelligence to accelerate claims during 2018's hurricane season. There are signs this push for speed could pose new headaches for the industry.

Last year marked the first widespread use of aerial technology to pinpoint damages and evaluate losses quickly as insurers scrambled to keep up with back-to-back hurricanes and wildfires.

But some insurers have reopened claims from that period because initial repair estimates turned out to be too low, according to executives and regulators.

Everest Re Group Ltd., which offers insurance coverage to other insurers, said last week it increased its estimated 2017 net catastrophe losses by $400 million partly because many customers had to reopen claims in Florida and Puerto Rico.

"Settling claims quickly was initially a sound strategy, but what we now know is that this approach left our clients vulnerable when the actual repair bills came in higher than at what they originally closed their claim," Chief Executive Dominic Addesso said on a conference call last week.

Insurers are testing a number of new tools as they try to speed up what still can be a clunky, time-consuming claims process. That includes everything from drones and small airplanes to assess damage without the help of an on-site adjuster to smartphone apps that let consumers submit their own damage photos. Some insurers are identifying property damage before policyholders even reach out to report a claim.

It took 16.5 days on average in 2017 for policyholders to receive payments for residential property damage after notifying their insurers, according to a J.D. Power survey released this year. That was down from 17.4 days in 2016 but still longer than the average time earlier in the decade.

Several large insurers said they are using flights, drones and aerial imagery more frequently in 2018. Allstate Corp. settled 16,500 claims this way in the first half of this year, compared with 12,600 for all of 2017.

Allstate also is expanding its use of a program called "Virtual Assist" that reduces the need for an adjuster to visit a site in person. Instead a service provider broadcasts the damage to an office-based adjuster via streaming video. In the first half of 2018, Allstate resolved 47,500 claims this way, up from 6,100 in all of 2017.

Virtual Assist addresses a problem that surfaced last year: a shortage of adjusters, which contributed to claims delays. The program eliminates travel time for adjusters, according to Allstate, allowing them to be more efficient.

American International Group Inc., another property insurer, launched a system last year with data feeds, analytics and forecasting models, so loss-prevention specialists can monitor wildfires and storms and their proximity to policyholders. AIG can then dispatch teams to clear brush or transport valuables.

"The name of the game today is preparation and mitigation as much as claim response afterward," said Jerry Hourihan, president of AIG's Private Client Group.

Some customers, like Stephen Dunn in Houston, are pleased with the industry's efforts to speed things up. He said a motion sensor installed on his doorbell alerted his phone to rising water levels during the 2017 arrival of Hurricane Harvey. He was able to take pictures remotely using the doorbell and send the photos to his insurer, USAA. Within two days USAA declared the cars in Mr. Dunn's garage a total loss and deposited money in his bank account.

"I had no idea that they would resolve this so quickly," he said.

Older policyholders have more reservations about some speed-enhancing tools, according to a 2017 study by J.D. Power. Policyholders older than 53 years who filed an auto claim via a photo app on a smartphone, tablet or computer were less satisfied than peers going the traditional route, according to J.D. Power. Younger customers, on the other hand, were more satisfied.

"Now the insurer is saying, 'You need to do the work. Be sure you photograph the damage and send us a photo,'" said David Pieffer, a J.D. Power insurance practice leader.

Do we still want to democratise finance?

Martin Arnold 
1,150 words
6 August 2018
10:01
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

P2P lenders fear new FCA proposals would deter retail investors

Britain s peer-to-peer lending industry fears being stripped of one of its key advantages, after the UK regulator proposed to block the access of many retail investors, alarming some senior executives in the nascent sector.

 This is a moment,  said Rhydian Lewis, chief executive of RateSetter   one of the UK s biggest peer-to-peer lending platforms.  They are looking to restrict this new industry and it is wrong. This is how things get stymied. 

Mr Lewis, who co-founded RateSetter in 2010 and has since overseen more than 2.6bn of consumer loans on the platform, said he would fight back by providing data to disprove the regulator s claim that peer-to-peer lenders are a high-risk investment.

His frustration stems fromthe consultation paperpublished just over a week ago by the Financial Conduct Authority, which proposed placing the same restrictions on peer-to-peer lenders that already apply to high-risk platforms, such as equity crowdfunding sites.

This would mean that to invest in peer-to-peer loans, investors would in future have to be certified as sophisticated, be very wealthy, be advised by an authorised person, or certify they will not invest more than 10 per cent of their net assets.

Mr Lewis said this contradicted the support the government and regulators have provided for the peer-to-peer lending sector, once hailed as a way to  democratise  finance by giving borrowers an alternative to banks and savers a way to earn more on their money.

Online platforms that match investors with borrowers, UK peer-to-peer lenders have grown rapidly, fuelled recently by last year s launch of Innovative Finance Individual Savings Accounts   government-approved, tax-free accounts.

In 2016, they increased net lending by 40 per cent, providing 1.23bn of loans to small businesses   equal to 15 per cent of all new SME loans by UK banks   and similar amounts of consumer and property loans, according to theCambridge Centre for Alternative Finance.

 On the one hand they are talking about the promotion of competition   the UK likes to be ahead on fintech   and on the other hand they have come to a fork in the road and they have chosen to be risk-averse,  said Mr Lewis.

The FCA s own impact assessment suggested its proposals could significantly change the industry. It said a survey of 4,500 investors in peer-to-peer loans found that four out of 10 had invested more than their annual income and a fifth had invested double that amount.

 We can infer from this that a significant number of them are likely to be putting more than 10 per cent of their net investible wealth into P2P lending,  the FCA said.  The proposals should make investors less likely to be overexposed to such assets and limit their potential losses. 

 We have also seen some poor business practices, particularly among some P2P platforms,  the FCA said. It cited a number of  potential and actual harms  for investors, including unreliable performance, disorderly failure, buying unsuitable products, poor customer treatment and setting prices too high or the quality of service too low.

Some smaller peer-to-peer lenders have collapsed under a cloud in recent years. In March, 21m of investors' money was put at risk when Manchester-based Collateral entered administration. Three years ago, Stockholm-listed TrustBuddy collapsed with a similar amount of investors  money trapped on its platform after finding some of it had gone missing.

The FCA signalled its main concern was to prepare for the next downturn.  So far, losses and defaults across the P2P sector have been low,  it said.  However, it is important to recognise that the sector is still relatively new and has not been through a full economic cycle. When economic conditions tighten, losses on loans and investments may increase. 

The regulator s plan could complicate the plans for an initial public offering at Funding Circle, the UK s biggest peer-to-peer lender that has made 3.8bn of loans to small businesses. But a person close to Funding Circle said the FCA proposals would benefit the largest platforms.

Paul Smee, chair of the Peer-to-Peer Finance Association, gave amixed responseto the consultation paper, saying:  Peer-to-peer lending needs to make its full contribution to the growth of the UK economy and we will be working to ensure that new regulatory requirements do not get in the way. 

Further fintech fascination

Data gold rushChina represents a giant playground for data-loving quantitative traders, given how much is readily available from vast, digital-savvy private companies and a state that is keen to take advantage of big data s benefits and relatively nonchalant over privacy. Even China s tech giants are getting involved. In December, search company Baidu partnered with E Fund Management, an asset manager, to create E Fund Yibai Intelligent Quantitative Strategy fund, which uses searches, news articles, social media and  Baidu s data-processing ability  to trade.

Backing up bitcoinIntercontinental Exchange, the world s second-largest exchange group by market value, has deepened its involvement in digital currencies with a new company that aims to make them mainstream for institutional investors and consumers. The Atlanta-based group announced plans to launch Bakkt, a business that plans to build infrastructure allowing easier use of digital currencies for everyday transactions.It s hard to buy coffee with bitcoin, but Starbucks may be poised to make that easier,Bloomberg reports.

Poacher turned gamekeeperHSBC, once the preferred bank of Mexican drug cartels, is now pitching its  know your customer software to rivals. The bank has agreed to sell its new compliance system, which combines robotic process automation and machine learning technologies to carry out automated checks on clients, through a deal with US-listed outsourcing company EXL.

Pro Private Markets
Fundraising Pulls Back as Investors Get Defensive; U.S. fundraising declines in the first half across a range of fund types, as investors become pickier about which groups they back.

By Chris Cumming, Tomio Geron and Laura Kreutzer 
2,062 words
6 August 2018
23:57
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Private-equity fundraising took a breather in the first half of the year, as flat public markets and fears of a slowdown caused investors to back off from the record-setting commitment pace of recent years.

The long private-equity fundraising boom didn't show any signs of stopping during the first six months of the year, but the level of investment tapered off from perhaps the highest ever to merely very good. Overall capital raised by private-equity and venture funds in the U.S. declined by a modest 13% to $173.05 billion from $199.92 billion during the same period a year earlier, according to LP Source, a data provider owned by WSJ Pro Private Equity publisher Dow Jones & Co. Capital raised dropped across nearly all major fund categories except for venture capital, and the overall number of fund closings dropped across the board.

Corporate Finance Dips in First Half as Megafunds Sit Out

The $121.25 billion raised by U.S. corporate-finance funds in the first half is a healthy sum by historical standards, but 15% lower than the 2017 pace, and the 210 funds that held closings represented a 14% drop, according to LP Source data.

Demand for private-equity funds is holding steady but isn't increasing as it did through 2016 and 2017. The stock market was flat over the first six months of 2018, which took pressure off limited partners to increase allocations to keep up with a rising market. Distributions have relaxed somewhat, reducing the urgency to keep pushing money out the door.

There also has been a subtle but clear shift by some LPs into a more risk-averse pose, industry observers said. Wary of signs of froth in the market, investors have become more selective when committing to new managers, and some have started to favor countercyclical strategies.

"We're starting to see an increased interest on the part of investors in defensive strategies," said Kevin Naughton, who co-leads the private-funds group at Credit Suisse Asset Management.

Fundraising declined across a broad swath of corporate-finance-fund types compared with the first half of 2017. Traditional buyout funds raised $53.24 billion, or 24% less capital than the same period last year. Growth and expansion funds raised 7% less than they did during the first half of 2017, while industry-focused funds rounded up 5% less. Meanwhile, capital collected for real-assets funds dropped 38%.

The only categories of U.S. corporate-finance funds that collected more money than they did a year earlier were restructuring and distressed-debt funds, which raised 3% more, and diversified private-equity funds, which raised 95% more. The latter category represents funds that invest across a broader capital structure than just buyouts, including venture and growth investments.

The popularity of distressed and diversified funds reflects concerns that high asset prices are making it harder for straight-down-the-middle buyout vehicles to earn the exceptional returns they once generated. Leverage multiples and underwriting criteria are becoming a bigger part of the due-diligence process, and LPs want to back sponsors that can unearth value in an overpriced market. As a result, so-called deep-value strategies, which target underpriced assets, and sponsors with specific operational expertise are becoming more in demand, placement agents said.

The largest distressed or restructuring fund to close in the first half was Strategic Value Partners' latest special-situations fund, which held a final closing on $2.85 billion, while the largest diversified private-equity fund to close was General Atlantic's General Atlantic Investment Partners 2017 LP, which closed with at least $3.29 billion.

There have been other surges of interest in distressed funds in recent years for instance, following the 2014 oil-price decline and after the late-2015 stock selloffs in the U.S. and China but managers often have struggled to access deals, as corporate defaults have remained low.

Nonetheless, after nearly a decade of economic expansion, many investors again feel confident that distressed funds will have their moment, if not this year then within the long-term time frame of a private-equity vehicle.

"Those that are interested in deep value and distressed are willing to sacrifice short-term performance for long-term outperformance," said Eric Zoller, founder of placement agent Sixpoint Partners.

Overall, the first half saw a lull in the fundraising cycle for the biggest managers, after Apollo Global Management LLC, KKR & Co., Silver Lake and others closed funds in the double-digit billions last year. The largest buyout fund that held a final closing in the first half of 2018 was American Securities' newest vehicle, which gathered $7 billion.

As the fundraising cycle accelerated in recent years and firms have shortened the interval between funds, re-ups with large, established firms have taken up a larger proportion of LPs' available cash. But when backing new groups, they often look for niche strategies such as ones with operational or sector specialties, or strategies that aren't correlated with broader markets, said Peter Martenson, a partner with placement agent Eaton Partners.

"If they're adding new strategies to developed programs, they're no longer going for broader generalist strategies," he said.

Along with an increase in operators raising funds, there has been a "continued resurgence" in venture and growth investing, said Mr. Martenson. For some LPs, high deal prices make quickly-expanding companies more appealing, Mr. Zoller said.

Growth-equity or expansion funds raised in the first half include Providence Equity Partners' Providence Strategic Growth III LP, which gathered $1.3 billion, and a $2.4 billion vehicle Thoma Bravo closed to back midmarket software deals.

However, there is some skepticism about tech buyouts, a sector many investors view as oversaturated. That isn't likely to stop the hottest players from raising new funds Thoma Bravo and Vista Equity Partners are both in the process of raising funds targeting more than $10 billion, for instance but many investors want to see more exits of recent big-money deals before committing more capital, placement agents said.

The private-debt market also strikes some LPs as oversaturated, with a number of firms having launched debt platforms in recent years. Private-debt fundraising fell 52% in the first half compared with the same period last year, and the number of closings declined by 26%. The bar for new investment in credit has risen simply because the field is so crowded with managers raising money, placement agents said.

Venture Capitalists Raise More Capital for Fewer Funds

Venture-capital firms continue to benefit from LP appetite for the latest hot startups this year as large offerings help drive fundraising totals.

During the first half, venture firms raised $26.66 billion, up about 19% from $22.33 billion in the first half of last year, driven partly by $6 billion raised for Sequoia Capital's global growth fund, according to LP Source. The overall number of funds raised declined by about 7%, however, to 297 funds during the first half from 321 offerings during the same period last year.

Fundraising accelerated in the second quarter with $17.74 billion raised, up 22% from $14.49 billion during the year-ago period. Even excluding Sequoia's fund, U.S. funds raised $11.74 billion in the quarter, which is the industry's largest quarterly haul since the second quarter of last year, when New Enterprise Associates closed its $3.3 billion fund.

Although industry veterans such as CRV, Meritech Capital Partners and Sequoia enjoyed fundraising success during the first half, investors also showed a willingness to bet on newer groups. At least 29 debut funds, often ones focused on very narrow strategies, have formed this year as of July 18, according to LP Source.

The increase of capital pouring into late-stage funds hasn't slowed down, thanks almost entirely to Sequoia's new fund. That fund accounted for the lion's share of the $7.46 billion raised by nine late-stage offerings during the first half.

Firms that invest across multiple venture-capital stages saw the overall number of funds that held closings increase more dramatically than capital raised. During the first half, 68 funds raised money, up 19% from 57 funds that held closings in last year's first half. Capital collected, however, only rose by 9% during those periods to $10.23 billion during the first six months of this year.

Early-stage funds collected $8.30 billion in the first half, slightly less than the $8.48 billion raised in the comparable period last year. However, the number of early-stage closings declined by 17% to 202 fund closings during the first half from 244 in the year-earlier period. Early-stage investors that raised funds this year include veteran firms 8VC, Matrix Partners, Emergence Capital, Madrona Venture Group, Redpoint Ventures and Venrock, as well as newcomer Unusual Ventures.

Despite the increasing amount of capital flowing into startups, LPs continue to seek out venture funds where they can find strong returns, said Tim Porter, a managing director at Madrona, which recently raised a $300 million early-stage fund for startups in the Pacific Northwest.

"The LP community is receptive, assuming you post strong returns," he said.

Large Secondary Firms Pause to Reload

Fundraising for secondary vehicles has cooled off after a few years of furious growth, with $9.18 billion raised by U.S. and European managers in the first half, down substantially from $17.49 billion raised during the first half of 2017. When calculating secondary fund totals, WSJ Pro Private Equity combines U.S. and European data because those managers often invest their funds globally.

The decline from last year's first half stems from the fact that few, if any, of the largest secondary firms held big closings during the first six months of this year.

The first half belonged largely to firms targeting smaller deals or specialized strategies, including HQ Capital, PineBridge Investments and RCP Advisors. Among the largest funds to raise capital during the first half were a $1.75 billion real-assets secondary fund from Blackstone Group LP's Strategic Partners unit and a new midmarket-focused vehicle being marketed by Deutsche Bank AG spinout Glendower Capital. Glendower closed on $1.3 billion for the fund, putting it about halfway to a $2.5 billion hard cap.

The secondary fundraising lull comes as secondary deal making continues to move at a record pace. During the first half, alone, secondary deal volume hit $27 billion, putting it on track to exceed the record level hit in 2017, according to intermediary Greenhill & Co.

But the fundraising lull may be short-lived. Firms that include Coller Capital, Ardian and Lexington Partners are pitching massive new funds to investors. Lexington, for one, is expected to hold a substantial closing of its next flagship fund before year-end, said people with knowledge of the firm.

Preparing for a Second-Half Bump

Although it was a relatively quiet first half for the biggest private-equity firms, and a crowded field for the smaller players, sponsors with creative fund strategies remain in demand. As the year continues to unfold, anticipated closings from larger players such as Thoma Bravo, Vista, TCV and Lexington could substantially boost totals by year-end. Early in the third quarter, for example, Insight Venture Partners closed its 10th tech-focused fund at $6.3 billion.

The largest firms still dominate the fundraising market, but as their returns fall, investors are seeking out niche strategies for the kind of home-run returns that large-cap funds no longer consistently offer, said Christopher Godfrey, president of private-equity analytics company Cepres GmbH.

"Investors tend to be more attracted to the brand names for security but are looking around the fringes for increased performance," he said.

This article appeared in the August 2018 issue of Private Equity Analyst, a monthly magazine published by WSJ Pro.

Markets
Insurers Are Speeding Up Claims. Their Worry: More Mistakes; Aerial technology such as drones was used widely last year to evaluate damage amid back-to-back disasters

By Leslie Scism and Nicole Friedman 
916 words
6 August 2018
15:48
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Property insurers are relying on more drones, small aircraft and artificial intelligence to accelerate claims during 2018's hurricane season. There are signs this push for speed could pose new headaches for the industry.

Last year marked the first widespread use of aerial technology to pinpoint damages and evaluate losses quickly as insurers scrambled to keep up with "https://www.wsj.com/articles/hurricanes-earthquakes-rack-up-huge-bill-for-insurers-1506945600" and wildfires.

But some insurers have reopened claims from that period because initial repair estimates turned out to be too low, according to executives and regulators.

Everest Re Group Ltd., which offers insurance coverage to other insurers, said last week it increased its estimated 2017 net catastrophe losses by $400 million partly because many customers had to reopen claims in Florida and Puerto Rico.

"Settling claims quickly was initially a sound strategy, but what we now know is that this approach left our clients vulnerable when the actual repair bills came in higher than at what they originally closed their claim," Chief Executive Dominic Addesso said on a conference call last week.

Insurers are testing a number of new tools as they try to speed up what still can be a clunky, time-consuming claims process. That includes everything from drones and small airplanes to assess damage without the help of an on-site adjuster to smartphone apps that let consumers submit their own damage photos. Some insurers are identifying property damage before policyholders even reach out to report a claim.

It took 16.5 days on average in 2017 for policyholders to receive payments for residential property damage after notifying their insurers, according to a J.D. Power survey released this year. That was down from 17.4 days in 2016 but still longer than the average time earlier in the decade.

Several large insurers said they are using flights, drones and aerial imagery more frequently in 2018. Allstate Corp. settled 16,500 claims this way in the first half of this year, compared with 12,600 for all of 2017.

Allstate is also expanding its use of a program called "Virtual Assist" that reduces the need for an adjuster to visit a site in person. Instead a service provider will broadcast the damage to an office-based adjuster via streaming video. In the first half of 2018 Allstate resolved 47,500 claims this way, up from 6,100 in all of 2017.

Virtual Assist addresses a problem that surfaced last year: a shortage of adjusters, which contributed to claims delays. The program eliminates travel time for adjusters, according to Allstate, allowing them to be more efficient.

American International Group Inc., another property insurer, launched a system last year with data feeds, analytics and forecasting models, so loss-prevention specialists can monitor wildfires and storms and their proximity to policyholders. AIG can then dispatch teams to clear brush or transport valuables.

"The name of the game today is preparation and mitigation as much as claim response afterward," said Jerry Hourihan, president of AIG's Private Client Group.

Some customers, like Stephen Dunn in Houston, are pleased with the industry's efforts to speed things up. He said a motion sensor installed on his doorbell alerted his phone to rising water levels during the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/two-months-after-harvey-houston-continues-to-count-the-cost-1509442203". He was able to take pictures remotely using the doorbell and send the photos to his insurer, USAA. Within two days USAA declared the cars in Mr. Dunn's garage a total loss and deposited money in his bank account.

"I had no idea that they would resolve this so quickly," he said.

Older policyholders have more reservations about some speed-enhancing tools, according to a 2017 study by J.D. Power. Policyholders older than 53 years who filed an auto claim via a photo app on a smartphone, tablet or computer were less satisfied than peers going the traditional route, according to J.D. Power. Younger customers, on the other hand, were more satisfied.

"Now the insurer is saying, 'You need to do the work. Be sure you photograph the damage and send us a photo,'" said David Pieffer, a J.D. Power insurance practice leader.

Some insurers are wary of using photo apps for home damage, citing the possibility of more complexities.

"We're cautious," said Ross Buchmueller, chief executive of PURE Group of Insurance Cos., which focuses on the affluent. "You don't want to find out you did an incomplete job and need to reopen the claim."

In Texas, for the vast majority of homeowners who filed claims following Hurricane Harvey's Aug. 25 landfall, it took an average of 24.5 days for resolution, according to state insurance-department data as of Oct. 31. In Florida, about half of complaints to an insurance hotline following Hurricane Irma involved claim delays.

In both states satisfaction with the handling of property claims fell last year, according to J.D. Power.

"We're seeing some improvements in the way that insurers are working with their customers to get the benefits paid with as little hassle as possible," said Amy Bach, executive director of consumer group United Policyholders. "But that is not all insurers and that is not in all regions."
